Title: How Does Climate Change Affect Agriculture? - Heifer International

# How Does Climate Change Affect Agriculture?

By Mark Chandler

\|

March 30, 2023


**Last Updated:** April 15, 2026

- [Share](https://www.facebook.com/sharer/sharer.php?u=https://www.heifer.org/blog/how-climate-change-affects-agriculture.html)
- [Post](https://twitter.com/intent/tweet?text=How%20Does%20Climate%20Change%20Affect%20Agriculture?&url=https://www.heifer.org/blog/how-climate-change-affects-agriculture.html&hashtags=heifer)
- [LinkedIn](https://www.linkedin.com/sharing/share-offsite/?url=https://www.heifer.org/blog/how-climate-change-affects-agriculture.html)

![](https://media-refresh.heifer.org/wp-content/uploads/20230116SC_1231.jpeg?or=0&q=60&fit=crop&crop=faces%2Centropy%2Ccenter&auto=compress%2Cformat&fm=jpeg&shad=50&w=767&h=512)

Climate change is an ever-present reality. Everyone on the planet feels the effects, but especially people in low-income countries and those who depend on specific climate conditions and natural resources to survive: the world’s smallholder farmers.

Farmers are already experiencing the effects of climate change, as extreme weather and less-predictable seasons transform pastures and croplands around the world. Climate change affects agriculture and those who rely on it by weakening environmental health, undermining production, wiping out crops, killing off livestock, making it more difficult to earn a living and extending the amount of time families must go without food.

Ensuring farming households have the tools they need to protect their livelihoods — and the environment — is key to our work at Heifer International.

Read on to learn more about how climate change affects agriculture and how Heifer’s “Caring for the Earth” approach to program development supports farmers in adapting to and mitigating the threats of the growing climate crisis.

## **The Link Between Climate Change and Agriculture**

Climate change and agriculture are intimately linked by deviations in weather patterns and temperatures. The world is [warming faster than ever](https://www.un.org/en/climatechange/science/causes-effects-climate-change) before, with a direct impact on agricultural production and the people who make their living from it.

The last two decades saw [the hottest temperatures since the 1800s](https://www.bloomberg.com/news/articles/2021-07-07/climate-change-linked-to-5-million-deaths-a-year-new-study-shows?leadSource=uverify%20wall), with the 10 warmest years in recorded history. Temperatures have risen by [1.1 degrees Celsius above pre-industrial levels](https://www.ipcc.ch/2021/08/09/ar6-wg1-20210809-pr/) as a result of global emissions.

![A man waters his garden in Senegal.](https://media-refresh.heifer.org/wp-content/uploads/20230116SC_1231-1.jpeg)Ismaila Gning waters his crops by hand in northern Senegal, where frequent drought conditions have made farming less reliable. Photo by Sylvain Cherkaoui/Heifer International.

Fossil fuels are the biggest contributor to climate change, responsible for [over three-quarters](https://www.un.org/en/climatechange/science/causes-effects-climate-change) of all global emissions. Burning oil, coal and gas releases large quantities of carbon dioxide, methane, nitrous oxide and other greenhouse gases. When these gases are emitted into the atmosphere, they trap heat close to the Earth’s surface, a consequence known as the greenhouse effect. Too many greenhouse gases result in global warming that causes climate change, which can also be thought of as shifts in Earth’s weather patterns over time.

In the past few decades, the world has seen and felt the consequences, with [smallholder farmers](https://www.heifer.org/blog/what-is-a-smallholder-farmer.html) — those who rear livestock or raise crops on a limited scale, on up to 10 hectares, or 25 acres, of land — on the front lines of this change.

![A woman feeds a flock of chickens while a man watches in India.](https://media-refresh.heifer.org/wp-content/uploads/IN202203171.jpeg)Sabitri Naik, left, and Upendranath Naik, right, feed the chickens at their home in Odisha, India. Many smallholder farmers are experiencing the worst of climate change but have the fewest resources to cope with its effects. Photo by Pranab K. Aich/Heifer International.

Farmers need healthy land and reliable weather patterns to maintain their livelihoods. But climate change is causing temperatures to rise and growing seasons to shift. There are more intense periods of rainfall, often followed by flooding, and longer dry spells, leading to prolonged drought. Natural disasters are becoming more frequent and severe. Ice caps are melting and [sea level rise is accelerating](https://www.nationalgeographic.com/environment/article/sea-level-rise-1), jeopardizing coastal habitats and fishing communities and leading to even more dangerous hurricanes and typhoons.

Agriculture itself, while necessary to feed the world’s growing population, has its own environmental impact and is also responsible for a share of the greenhouse gas emissions contributing to climate change. However, people in lesser developed countries, where many smallholder farmers earn their living, have historically contributed the least to climate change yet are immensely affected by its impacts.

In short, the effect of climate change on agriculture is enormous — with outsize consequences for small-scale farmers.

## **How Climate Change Affects Agriculture**

Extreme weather and shifting seasons are a direct threat to farmers’ livelihoods and well-being in multiple ways.

### **Heightened Food Insecurity**

As dry seasons get longer and water becomes scarcer, farmers aren’t able to grow or raise the food they rely on to earn an income and feed their families. In the Horn of Africa, for example, farmers have experienced [five failed rainy seasons](https://www.reuters.com/world/africa/horn-africa-track-5th-failed-rainy-season-wmo-2022-08-26/) in a row, and [over 80 million people](https://www.reuters.com/world/africa/horn-africa-track-5th-failed-rainy-season-wmo-2022-08-26/) in the region are estimated to be food insecure as a result of climate change.

Meanwhile, rising temperatures can also lead to spoilage and contamination, compromising the quality of goods. As disruptions to food production increase, the [1 in 3 people](https://www.fao.org/state-of-food-security-nutrition/2021/en/) who already don’t have enough to eat will suffer most.

### **Land Degradation and Water Scarcity**

[Changes in rainfall patterns](https://www.heifer.org/blog/no-more-praying-for-rain-bringing-water-to-nepals-waterless.html), deforestation and overgrazing have detrimental

![A close-up of a hand planting a seedling.](https://media-refresh.heifer.org/wp-content/uploads/20230116SC_157-300x200.jpeg)Climate change drastically affects the quality of the land required to grow crops and pasture. Photo by Sylvain Cherkaoui/Heifer International.

and often irreparable effects on agriculture. Farmers are struggling to keep their animals healthy in drier, more extreme conditions. Lack of water and warmer temperatures make it harder for crops to grow, and soil is drying to dust. Land is gradually becoming less productive and more vulnerable to future shocks like drought, and competition and conflict over resources is increasing as farmers fight for claim to arable land and access to clean water.

### **Reduced Yield**

Farmers are reporting record-breaking losses. It’s predicted global food yields could decline [by as much as 30% by 2050](https://unfoundation.org/blog/post/climate-change-and-the-future-of-food/) if farmers aren’t able to adapt to the effects of climate change. These disruptions are not just a challenge for farmers — climate change affects the entire agriculture sector, leading to higher food prices and decreased availability of food worldwide.

### **Increased Poverty**

Climate change has a direct impact on farmers’ livelihoods: When crops and livestock are lost, so is income. Farmers in the world’s most vulnerable regions often already live at the edge of poverty, and the risk is growing as climate change accelerates. It’s estimated that [43 million people in Africa alone](https://www.worldbank.org/en/news/feature/2022/10/17/what-you-need-to-know-about-food-security-and-climate-change) could be pushed into poverty by 2030 as crop yields continue to decline.

### **Migration and Displacement**

Climate change also affects agriculture by destroying farms or making

![A group of people work with shovels on the forest floor in Guatemala.](https://media-refresh.heifer.org/wp-content/uploads/GT202103_6944-300x200.jpeg)Heifer applies a Caring for the Earth approach in its global programs. Above, members of a Heifer-trained disaster reduction team work in a Chisec forest in Guatemala, which is highly vulnerable to natural disasters that threaten agricultural communities. Photo by Daniel Lopez/Heifer International.

environmental conditions so challenging that farming is no longer an option. Every year, a growing number of farming families are forced to leave their farms and fields in search of new work. In 2021, extreme weather uprooted [more than 23 million people](https://www.internal-displacement.org/global-report/grid2022/) globally.

Additionally, in the world’s most vulnerable countries, conflict and competition over natural resources are increasing as climate change intensifies. When conflict breaks out, damage and destruction breaks up communities, drives farmers from their homes and increases the likelihood of unemployment.

## **The Future for Climate Change and Agriculture**

According to the Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change, global warming must be limited to 1.5 degrees Celsius to avoid catastrophic-level drought, crop failures and other impacts on human, animal and planetary health — but [new research](https://www.ipcc.ch/report/ar6/syr/) predicts the world will reach this threshold within the next 12 years without an immediate and significant reduction in greenhouse gas emissions. By the end of the century, climate scientists warn the planet could warm by [more than 4 degrees Celsius and sea levels could rise by more than a meter](https://climate.selectra.com/en/news/ipcc-report-2022).

Even the rise of 1.5 degrees Celsius is not considered safe, but [every additional tenth of a degree](https://www.who.int/news-room/fact-sheets/detail/climate-change-and-health) of warming can make a difference for human and animal health — and for farmers’ ability to continue producing sufficient food.

![A woman serves milk to her two young children outdoors, surrounded by banana plants.](https://media-refresh.heifer.org/wp-content/uploads/TZ202212-001-614.jpeg)Rebecka Enock Mwangurube pours milk from her dairy cows for two of her six children in southwestern Tanzania. When climate change makes it more difficult to raise crops and animals, those who rely on their own farms for nutrition are especially at risk of food shortages. Photo by Moshi J. Lukindo/Heifer International.

While heat intensifies and climate change accelerates, smallholder farming families will face even greater obstacles and challenging working conditions. It will mean scarcer natural resources, higher chance of crop failure and livestock loss, greater threat of food insecurity, and millions of lives and livelihoods at risk. The stakes are higher than ever before, making it imperative we address the climate crisis and implement climate-smart solutions.

## **Reducing How Climate Change Affects Agriculture**

As the world confronts the triple challenge of needing to produce more healthy food, with less ecological impact, in an increasingly difficult growing environment, engaging farmers is our first line of defense. With the right tools and training, farmers can earn a sustainable living income in agriculture — and be at the forefront of protecting our planet.

Heifer is using the Caring for the Earth lens across our global programs, looking at how we can increase the stability of the communities we work with, the farmlands that support them and their livestock — actively analyzing how we can improve the resilience of these essential systems given the urgency and need.

We are integrating climate-smart practices, taking time to understand smallholder farmers’ unique challenges and help them adopt new methods and technologies that build resilience and protect precious natural resources, like soil and water.

Our Caring for the Earth programmatic approach enables farmers to remain on

![A woman examines grass in a pasture in Honduras.](https://media-refresh.heifer.org/wp-content/uploads/HN202208-464-200x300.jpeg)Keyla Aparicio inspects her pasture in Honduras, where Heifer works with livestock farmers to utilize rotational grazing, a method of land management that regenerates grasslands and helps farmers reduce their environmental impact. Photo by Phillip Davis/Heifer International.

their land, [adapt to climate threats](https://www.heifer.org/blog/derisking-nigerias-climate-threatened-farmlands.html) and increase their productivity and income, at the same time promoting [regenerative land management practices](https://www.heifer.org/blog/what-is-regenerative-agriculture.html) that mitigate the effects of climate change. Alongside local farmers and their communities, we work to [restore soil nutrients](https://www.heifer.org/blog/heal-the-soil-heal-the-world-protecting-earths-tiniest-beasts.html), retain needed moisture in fields and devise strategies to [minimize waste](https://www.heifer.org/blog/waste-to-wonder-5-ways-heifer-farmers-use-byproducts.html) and keep soil, rivers and lakes uncontaminated.

Additionally, we help [livestock farmers manage the size of their flocks and herds](https://www.heifer.org/blog/how-rilli-grew-her-herd.html). By improving breed quality and [raising healthier animals](https://www.heifer.org/blog/healthy-animals-healthy-people-heifer-experts-weigh-in-on-what-makes-a-thriving-farm-ecosystem.html), farmers are able to reduce the strain on vital local resources and protect their land for future generations, while earning better prices for their animals and outputs.

We also know there is much more to be done to address the challenges related to climate change and agriculture, and we are striving to find more innovative solutions and new ways to partner with farmers to build resilience and regenerate a healthy planet for all.

Together, we’re working toward a sustainable future, helping smallholder farmers build income-generating farms while protecting and restoring the environment and ensuring long-term, [resilient food production](https://www.heifer.org/blog/food-smart-climate-smart-4-ways-heifer-promotes-sustainable-food-around-the-world.html) for the world.

Title: Climate Impacts on Agriculture and Food Supply - US EPA
[Jump to main content](https://19january2017snapshot.epa.gov/climate-impacts/climate-impacts-agriculture-and-food-supply_.html#main-content)

This is not the current EPA website. To navigate to the current EPA website, please go to [www.epa.gov](https://www.epa.gov/). This website is historical material reflecting the EPA website as it existed on January 19, 2017. This website is no longer updated and links to external websites and some internal pages may not work. [More information](https://19january2017snapshot.epa.gov/climate-impacts/snapshot-help.html) »

[Home](https://19january2017snapshot.epa.gov/) [Menu](https://19january2017snapshot.epa.gov/climate-impacts/climate-impacts-agriculture-and-food-supply_.html#mobile-links)

## Climate Change Impacts

# Climate Impacts on Agriculture and Food Supply

### ![Sunset over an agricultural field](https://19january2017snapshot.epa.gov/sites/production/files/styles/medium/public/2016-01/agriculture_small.png)On This Page:

- [Overview](https://19january2017snapshot.epa.gov/climate-impacts/climate-impacts-agriculture-and-food-supply_.html#Overview)
- [Impacts on Crops](https://19january2017snapshot.epa.gov/climate-impacts/climate-impacts-agriculture-and-food-supply_.html#crops)
- [Impacts on Livestock](https://19january2017snapshot.epa.gov/climate-impacts/climate-impacts-agriculture-and-food-supply_.html#livestock)
- [Impacts on Fisheries](https://19january2017snapshot.epa.gov/climate-impacts/climate-impacts-agriculture-and-food-supply_.html#fisheries)
- [International Impacts](https://19january2017snapshot.epa.gov/climate-impacts/climate-impacts-agriculture-and-food-supply_.html#international)

* * *

## Overview

## Key Points

- Moderate warming and more carbon dioxide in the atmosphere may help some plants to grow faster. However, more severe warming, floods, and drought may reduce yields.

- Livestock may be at risk, both directly from heat stress and indirectly from reduced quality of their food supply.

- Fisheries will be affected by changes in water temperature that make waters more hospitable to invasive species and shift the ranges or lifecycle timing of certain fish species.


Agriculture is an important sector of the U.S. economy. The crops, livestock, and seafood produced  in the United States contribute more than $300 billion to the economy each year. [\[1\]](https://19january2017snapshot.epa.gov/climate-impacts/climate-impacts-agriculture-and-food-supply_.html#ref1)  When food-service and other agriculture-related industries are included, the agricultural and food sectors contribute more than $750 billion to the gross domestic product. [\[2\]](https://19january2017snapshot.epa.gov/climate-impacts/climate-impacts-agriculture-and-food-supply_.html#ref2)

Agriculture and fisheries are highly dependent on the climate. Increases in temperature and carbon dioxide (CO2) can increase some crop yields in some places. But to realize these benefits, nutrient levels, soil moisture, water availability, and other conditions must also be met. Changes in the frequency and severity of droughts and floods could pose challenges for farmers and ranchers and threaten food safety. [\[3\]](https://19january2017snapshot.epa.gov/climate-impacts/climate-impacts-agriculture-and-food-supply_.html#ref3)   Meanwhile, warmer water temperatures are likely to cause the habitat ranges of many fish and shellfish species to shift, which could disrupt [ecosystems](https://19january2017snapshot.epa.gov/climate-impacts/climate-impacts-ecosystems). Overall, climate change could make it more difficult to grow crops, raise animals, and catch fish in the same ways and same places as we have done in the past. The effects of climate change also need to be considered along with other evolving factors that affect agricultural production, such as changes in farming practices and technology.

## Impacts on Crops

[![Line graph that shows the yield in bushels per acre by year. The map calls out several events that caused significant declines in yield. Blight, wet spring and early frost, droughts, flood, and unusual climate events caused as much as 29 percent declines in yield. The data ranges from 1960 to 2010 and over that period of time the trend shows that the yield per acre has risen from approximately 60 to 150 bushels per acre.](https://19january2017snapshot.epa.gov/sites/production/files/styles/medium/public/2016-08/uscornyields-large.jpg)](https://19january2017snapshot.epa.gov/sites/production/files/2016-08/uscornyields-large.jpg) Despite technological improvements that increase corn yields, extreme weather events have caused significant yield reductions in some years. Source: [USGCRP (2009)](http://www.globalchange.gov/components/com_joomgallery/img_originals/global_climate_change_impacts_in_the_united_states_1/agriculture_7/agriculture_5_20090708_1595135882.jpg)

Click the image to view a larger version.

Crops grown in the United States are critical for the food supply here and around the world. U.S. farms supply nearly 25% of all grains (such as wheat, corn, and rice) on the global market.\[4\] Changes in temperature, atmospheric carbon dioxide (CO2), and the frequency and intensity of extreme weather could have significant impacts on crop yields.

For any particular crop, the effect of increased temperature will depend on the crop's optimal temperature for growth and reproduction. [\[1\]](https://19january2017snapshot.epa.gov/climate-impacts/climate-impacts-agriculture-and-food-supply_.html#ref1) In some areas, warming may benefit the types of crops that are typically planted there, or allow farmers to shift to crops that are currently grown in warmer areas. Conversely, if the higher temperature exceeds a crop's optimum temperature, yields will decline.

## Related Links

EPA


- [National Agriculture Center](https://19january2017snapshot.epa.gov/agriculture)
- [Student's Guide to Climate Change: Agriculture](https://www3.epa.gov/climatechange/kids/impacts/effects/agriculture.html)

Other:



- [National Climate Assessment: Agriculture](http://nca2014.globalchange.gov/report/sectors/agriculture)
- [USDA: Agriculture and Climate Change](http://www.usda.gov/oce/climate_change/effects_2012/effects_agriculture.htm)
- [IPCC: Fifth Assessment Report - Food Security and Food Production Systems](http://www.ipcc.ch/report/ar5/wg2/)

- Higher CO2 levels can affect crop yields. Some laboratory experiments suggest that elevated CO2 levels can increase plant growth.  However, other factors, such as changing temperatures, ozone, and water and nutrient constraints, may counteract these potential increases in yield. For example, if temperature exceeds a crop's optimal level, if sufficient water and nutrients are not available, yield increases may be reduced or reversed. Elevated CO2 has been associated with reduced protein and nitrogen content in alfalfa and soybean plants, resulting in a loss of quality.  Reduced grain and forage quality can reduce the ability of pasture and rangeland to support grazing livestock. [\[1\]](https://19january2017snapshot.epa.gov/climate-impacts/climate-impacts-agriculture-and-food-supply_.html#ref1)
- More extreme temperature and precipitation can prevent crops from growing. Extreme events, especially floods and droughts, can harm crops and reduce yields. For example, in 2010 and 2012, high nighttime temperatures affected corn yields across the U.S. Corn Belt, and premature budding due to a warm winter caused $220 million in losses of Michigan cherries in 2012. [\[1\]](https://19january2017snapshot.epa.gov/climate-impacts/climate-impacts-agriculture-and-food-supply_.html#ref1)
- Dealing with drought could become a challenge in areas where rising summer temperatures cause soils to become drier. Although increased irrigation might be possible in some places, in other places water supplies may also be reduced, leaving less water available for irrigation when more is needed.
- Many weeds, pests, and fungi thrive under warmer temperatures, wetter climates, and increased CO2 levels. Currently, U.S. farmers spend more than $11 billion per year to fight weeds, which compete with crops for light, water, and nutrients. [\[1\]](https://19january2017snapshot.epa.gov/climate-impacts/climate-impacts-agriculture-and-food-supply_.html#ref1) The ranges and distribution of weeds and pests are likely to increase with climate change. This could cause new problems for farmers' crops previously unexposed to these species.
- Though rising CO2 can stimulate plant growth, it also reduces the nutritional value of most food crops. Rising levels of atmospheric carbon dioxide reduce the concentrations of protein and essential minerals in most plant species, including wheat, soybeans, and rice. This direct effect of rising CO2 on the nutritional value of crops represents a potential threat to human health. Human health is also threatened by increased pesticide use due to increased pest pressures and reductions in the efficacy of pesticides. [\[3\]](https://19january2017snapshot.epa.gov/climate-impacts/climate-impacts-agriculture-and-food-supply_.html#ref3)

[Top of Page](https://19january2017snapshot.epa.gov/climate-impacts/climate-impacts-agriculture-and-food-supply_.html#main-content)

## Impacts on Livestock

[![Livestock by location in the United States 2012. Chickens are most common in the southeast, hogs and pigs in the upper midwest, and cattle and calves across the Great Plains.](https://19january2017snapshot.epa.gov/sites/production/files/styles/medium/public/2016-08/livestock-2012.png)](https://19january2017snapshot.epa.gov/sites/production/files/2016-08/livestock-2012.png) Livestock locations in the continental United States. Source: [USGCRP (2016)](http://www.globalchange.gov/components/com_joomgallery/img_originals/global_climate_change_impacts_in_the_united_states_1/agriculture_7/agriculture_8_20090708_1964800281.jpg)

Click the image to view a larger version.Americans consume more than 36 million metric tons of meat and poultry annually. [\[4\]](https://19january2017snapshot.epa.gov/climate-impacts/climate-impacts-agriculture-and-food-supply_.html#ref4) Livestock and poultry account for over half of U.S. agricultural cash receipts, often over $100 billion per year. [\[5\]](https://19january2017snapshot.epa.gov/climate-impacts/climate-impacts-agriculture-and-food-supply_.html#ref5) Changes in climate could affect animals both directly and indirectly.

- Heat waves, which are projected to increase under climate change, could directly threaten livestock. In 2011, exposure to high temperature events caused over $1 billion in heat-related losses to agricultural producers. [\[1\]](https://19january2017snapshot.epa.gov/climate-impacts/climate-impacts-agriculture-and-food-supply_.html#ref1) Heat stress affects animals both directly and indirectly. Over time, heat stress can increase vulnerability to disease, reduce fertility, and reduce milk production.
- Drought may threaten pasture and feed supplies. Drought reduces the amount of quality forage available to grazing livestock. Some areas could experience longer, more intense droughts, resulting from higher summer temperatures and reduced precipitation. For animals that rely on grain, changes in crop production due to drought could also become a problem.
- Climate change may increase the prevalence of parasites and diseases that affect livestock. The earlier onset of spring and warmer winters could allow some parasites and pathogens to survive more easily. In areas with increased rainfall, moisture-reliant pathogens could thrive. [\[6\]](https://19january2017snapshot.epa.gov/climate-impacts/climate-impacts-agriculture-and-food-supply_.html#ref6)
- Potential changes in veterinary practices, including an increase in the use of parasiticides and other animal health treatments, are likely to be adopted to maintain livestock health in response to climate-induced changes in pests, parasites, and microbes. This could increase the risk of pesticides entering the food chain or lead to evolution of pesticide resistance, with subsequent implications for the safety, distribution, and consumption of livestock and aquaculture products. [\[3\]](https://19january2017snapshot.epa.gov/climate-impacts/climate-impacts-agriculture-and-food-supply_.html#ref3)
- Increases in carbon dioxide (CO2) may increase the productivity of pastures, but may also decrease their quality. Increases in atmospheric CO2 can increase the productivity of plants on which livestock feed. However, the quality of some of the forage found in pasturelands decreases with higher CO2. As a result, cattle would need to eat more to get the same nutritional benefits.

[Top of Page](https://19january2017snapshot.epa.gov/climate-impacts/climate-impacts-agriculture-and-food-supply_.html#main-content)

## Impacts on Fisheries

American fishermen catch or harvest five million metric tons of fish and shellfish each year. [\[7\]](https://19january2017snapshot.epa.gov/climate-impacts/climate-impacts-agriculture-and-food-supply_.html#ref7) U.S. fisheries contribute more than $1.55 billion to the economy annually (as of 2012). [\[8\]](https://19january2017snapshot.epa.gov/climate-impacts/climate-impacts-agriculture-and-food-supply_.html#ref8) Many fisheries already face multiple stresses, including overfishing and water pollution. Climate change may worsen these stresses. In particular, temperature changes could lead to significant impacts.

[![Map showing American lobster, Red hake, black sea bass migration up the East coast based on climate change influencesSince the late 1960s, the three species have moved northward by an average of 119 miles.](https://19january2017snapshot.epa.gov/sites/production/files/styles/large/public/2016-08/fisheries-location.png)](https://19january2017snapshot.epa.gov/sites/production/files/2016-08/fisheries-location.png) This map shows the annual centers of biomass for three species in the northeastern United States from 1968 to 2015. Dots are shaded from light to dark to show change over time. Source: [US EPA (2016). _Climate Change Indicators in the United States: Marine Species Distribution_](https://19january2017snapshot.epa.gov/climate-indicators/marine-species-distribution). Data Source: NOAA (2016). [OceanAdapt](http://oceanadapt.rutgers.edu/). [Exit](https://19january2017snapshot.epa.gov/home/exit-epa "EPA's External Link Disclaimer")

Click the image to view a larger version.The ranges of many fish and shellfish species may change. In waters off the northeastern United States, several economically important species have shifted northward since the late 1960s. The three species shown in \[the figure to the left\]  (American lobster, red hake, and black sea bass) have moved northward by an average of 119 miles. [\[9\]](https://19january2017snapshot.epa.gov/climate-impacts/climate-impacts-agriculture-and-food-supply_.html#ref9)

-  Many aquatic species can find colder areas of streams and lakes or move north along the coast or in the ocean. Nevertheless, moving into new areas may put these species into competition with other species over food and other resources, as explained on the [Ecosystems Impacts](https://19january2017snapshot.epa.gov/climate-impacts/climate-impacts-ecosystems) page.
- Some marine disease outbreaks have been linked with changing climate.  Higher water temperatures and higher estuarine salinities have enabled an oyster parasite to spread farther north along the Atlantic coast.  Winter warming in the Arctic is contributing to salmon diseases in the Bering Sea and a resulting reduction in the Yukon Chinook Salmon, Finally, warmer temperatures have caused disease outbreaks in coral, eelgrass, and abalone.[\[3\]](https://19january2017snapshot.epa.gov/climate-impacts/climate-impacts-agriculture-and-food-supply_.html#ref3), [\[10\]](https://19january2017snapshot.epa.gov/climate-impacts/climate-impacts-agriculture-and-food-supply_.html#ref10)
- Changes in temperature and seasons can affect the timing of reproduction and migration. Many steps within an aquatic animal's lifecycle are controlled by temperature and the changing of the seasons. For example, in the Northwest warmer water temperatures may affect the lifecycle of salmon and increase the likelihood of disease. Combined with other climate impacts, these effects are projected to lead to large declines in salmon populations.[\[1\]](https://19january2017snapshot.epa.gov/climate-impacts/climate-impacts-agriculture-and-food-supply_.html#ref1), [\[11\]](https://19january2017snapshot.epa.gov/climate-impacts/climate-impacts-agriculture-and-food-supply_.html#ref11), [\[12\]](https://19january2017snapshot.epa.gov/climate-impacts/climate-impacts-agriculture-and-food-supply_.html#ref12)

In addition to warming, the [world's oceans](https://19january2017snapshot.epa.gov/climate-impacts/climate-impacts-coastal-areas) are gradually becoming more acidic due to increases in atmospheric carbon dioxide (CO2). Increasing acidity could harm shellfish by weakening their shells, which are created by removing calcium from seawater. [\[10\]](https://19january2017snapshot.epa.gov/#ref1) Acidification also threatens the structures of sensitive ecosystems upon which some fish and shellfish rely. [\[1\]](https://19january2017snapshot.epa.gov/climate-impacts/climate-impacts-agriculture-and-food-supply_.html#ref1), [\[13\]](https://19january2017snapshot.epa.gov/climate-impacts/climate-impacts-agriculture-and-food-supply_.html#ref13)

![Process Diagram: Impact pathway of carbon dioxide emissions on the shellfish market](https://19january2017snapshot.epa.gov/sites/production/files/styles/large/public/2016-08/ocean-acidification-impact-shellfish.png)This diagram shows the impact pathway of carbon dioxide emissions on the shellfish market.  Carbon dioxide is absorbed by oceans, resulting in ocean acidification. Acidification reduces the size and abundance of shellfish, which in turn leads to decreased harvest and eventually to changes in prices for consumers. Source: US EPA (2015). [_Climate Change in the United States: Benefits of Global Action_](https://19january2017snapshot.epa.gov/cira)

## International Impacts

Climate change is very likely to affect food security at the global, regional, and local level. Climate change can disrupt food availability, reduce access to food, and affect food quality. [\[14\]](https://19january2017snapshot.epa.gov/climate-impacts/climate-impacts-agriculture-and-food-supply_.html#ref14)  For example, projected increases in temperatures, changes in precipitation patterns, changes in extreme weather events, and reductions in water availability may all result in reduced agricultural productivity. Increases in the frequency and severity extreme weather events can also interrupt food delivery, and resulting spikes in food prices after extreme events are expected to be more frequent in the future.  Increasing temperatures can contribute to spoilage and contamination.

[Internationally](https://19january2017snapshot.epa.gov/climate-impacts/international-climate-impacts), these effects of climate change on agriculture and food supply are likely to be similar to those seen in the United States. However, other stressors such as population growth may magnify the effects of climate change on food security. In developing countries, adaptation options like changes in crop-management or ranching practices, or improvements to irrigation are more limited than in the United States and other industrialized nations.

Any climate-related disturbance to food distribution and transport, internationally or domestically, may have significant impacts not only on safety and quality but also on food access. For example, the food transportation system in the United States frequently moves large volumes of grain by water. In the case of an extreme weather event affecting a waterway, there are few, if any, alternate pathways for transport.  High temperatures and a shortage of rain in the summer of 2012 led to one of the most severe summer droughts the nation has seen and posed serious impacts to the Mississippi River watershed, a major transcontinental shipping route for Midwestern agriculture. This drought resulted in significant food and economic losses due to reductions in barge traffic, the volume of goods carried, and the number of Americans employed by the tugboat industry. The 2012 drought was immediately followed by flooding throughout the Mississippi in the spring of 2013, which also resulted in disruptions of barge traffic and food transport.[\[3\]](https://19january2017snapshot.epa.gov/climate-impacts/climate-impacts-agriculture-and-food-supply_.html#ref3) Transportation changes such as these reduce the ability of farmers to export their grains to international markets, and can affect global food prices.

Impacts to the global food supply concern the United States because food shortages can cause humanitarian crises and national security concerns.  They also can increase domestic food prices.

[Top of Page](https://19january2017snapshot.epa.gov/climate-impacts/climate-impacts-agriculture-and-food-supply_.html#main-content)

Title: Climate change cuts global crop yields, even when farmers adapt
Main content start

[Agriculture](https://sustainability.stanford.edu/news/research/land-water/agriculture), [Climate change](https://sustainability.stanford.edu/news/research/climate/climate-change), [Climate impacts](https://sustainability.stanford.edu/news/research/climate/climate-impacts)

# Climate change cuts global crop yields, even when farmers adapt

A sweeping new analysis finds that rising global temperatures will dampen the world’s capacity to produce food from most staple crops, even after accounting for economic development and adaptation by farmers.

June 18, 2025

By

Josie Garthwaite

[Stanford Facebook](http://www.facebook.com/sharer.php?u=https://sustainability.stanford.edu/news/climate-change-cuts-global-crop-yields-even-when-farmers-adapt&display=popup)

[Stanford Twitter](https://twitter.com/intent/tweet?url=https://sustainability.stanford.edu/news/climate-change-cuts-global-crop-yields-even-when-farmers-adapt&text=)

[Stanford LinkedIn](https://www.linkedin.com/shareArticle?mini=true&url=https://sustainability.stanford.edu/news/climate-change-cuts-global-crop-yields-even-when-farmers-adapt&title=&summary=)

[Forward Email](mailto:?subject=Climate%20change%20cuts%20global%20crop%20yields,%20even%20when%20farmers%20adapt%20|%20Stanford%20Doerr%20School%20of%20Sustainability&body=https://sustainability.stanford.edu/news/climate-change-cuts-global-crop-yields-even-when-farmers-adapt)

[Print Article](https://sustainability.stanford.edu/news/climate-change-cuts-global-crop-yields-even-when-farmers-adapt)

![Aerial view of agricultural fields](https://sustainability.stanford.edu/sites/sustainability/files/styles/card_1900x950/public/media/image/gettyimages-2148055154_0.jpg?h=c7c84225&itok=FfJWnPyI)

Aerial view of agricultural fields. _(Image credit: Getty Images)_

The global food system faces growing risks from climate change, even as farmers seek to adapt, according to a June 18 study in [_Nature_](https://doi.org/10.1038/s41586-025-09085-w "(opens in a new window)").

In contrast to previous studies suggesting that warming could increase global food production, the researchers estimate that every additional degree Celsius of global warming on average will drag down the world’s ability to produce food by 120 calories per person per day, or 4.4% of current daily consumption.

![Solomon Hsiang portrait](https://sustainability.stanford.edu/sites/sustainability/files/styles/stanford_circle/public/media/image/solomonhsiang-portrait_0.jpeg.png?itok=TO4MOgqj)

Solomon Hsiang is a professor in the global environmental policy area of the Stanford Doerr School of Sustainability’s Department of Environmental Social Sciences. _(Image credit: Drew Bird)_

“When global production falls, consumers are hurt because prices go up and it gets harder to access food and feed our families,” said [Solomon Hsiang](https://profiles.stanford.edu/solomon-hsiang "(opens in a new window)"), professor of environmental social sciences at the [Stanford Doerr School of Sustainability](http://sustainability.stanford.edu/) and a senior author of the study. “If the climate warms by 3 degrees, that’s basically like everyone on the planet giving up breakfast.” That’s a high cost for a world where [more than 800 million people](https://www.who.int/news/item/24-07-2024-hunger-numbers-stubbornly-high-for-three-consecutive-years-as-global-crises-deepen--un-report "(opens in a new window)") at times go a day or more without food because of inadequate access.

The projected losses for U.S. agriculture are especially steep. “Places in the Midwest that are really well suited for present day corn and soybean production just get hammered under a high warming future,” said lead study author [Andrew Hultgren](https://www.andrewhultgren.com/ "(opens in a new window)"), an assistant professor of [agricultural and consumer economics](https://ace.illinois.edu/ "(opens in a new window)") at the [University of Illinois Urbana-Champaign](https://illinois.edu/ "(opens in a new window)"). “You do start to wonder if the Corn Belt is going to be the Corn Belt in the future.”

Hsiang and Hultgren worked on the analysis with more than a dozen scholars over the past eight years as a project with [Climate Impact Lab](http://impactlab.org/ "(opens in a new window)"), a research consortium that Hsiang co-directs with University of Chicago economist Michael Greenstone, Rutgers University climate scientist Robert Kopp, and climate policy expert Trevor Houser of the Rhodium Group.

“This is basically like sending our agricultural profits overseas. We will be sending benefits to producers in Canada, Russia, China. Those are the winners, and we in the U.S. are the losers,” said Hsiang. “The longer we wait to reduce emissions, the more money we lose.”

## Limits to adaptation

![Maps depict projected end-of-century change in crop yields](https://sustainability.stanford.edu/sites/sustainability/files/styles/large/public/media/image/hultgren_maps_0.png?itok=57lwpfGP)

Projected end-of-century change in crop yields resulting from climate change in a scenario where emissions remain high, accounting for adaptation to climate and increasing incomes. _Credit: Adapted from Hultgren et al. (Nature, 2025)_

The study draws on observations from more than 12,000 regions across 55 countries. The team analyzed adaptation costs and yields for crops that provide two-thirds of humanity’s calories: wheat, corn, rice, soybeans, barley, and cassava.

Previous studies failed to account for realistic adaptation by farmers, assuming either “perfect” adaptation or none at all. The new study is the first to systematically measure how much farmers adjust to changing conditions. In many regions, for example, they switch crop varieties, shift planting and harvesting dates, or alter fertilizer use.

The team estimates these adjustments offset about one-third of climate-related losses in 2100 if emissions continue to rise, but the rest remain. “Any level of warming, even when accounting for adaptation, results in global output losses from agriculture,” said Hultgren.

The steepest losses occur at the extremes of the agricultural economy: in modern breadbaskets that now enjoy some of the world’s best growing conditions, and in subsistence farming communities relying on small harvests of cassava. In terms of food production capacity from staple crops, the analysis finds yield losses may average 41% in the wealthiest regions and 28% in the lowest income regions by 2100.

The modeling points to a 50% chance that global rice yields will increase on a hotter planet, largely because rice benefits from warmer nights, while the odds that yields will decline by century’s end range from roughly 70% to 90% for each of the other staple crops.

## Higher emissions bring bigger losses

![Chart shows decline in global food production capacity as global surface temperatures rise. ](https://sustainability.stanford.edu/sites/sustainability/files/styles/large/public/media/image/hultgren_calories_0.png?itok=FhC0raNh)

Global warming exceeding 2 degrees Celsius above the 2001-2010 average would likely cut global food production capacity from six staple crops by nearly a quarter. _Credit: Adapted from Hultgren et al. (Nature, 2025)_

With the planet already about 1.5 degrees Celsius hotter than pre-industrial levels, farmers in many areas are experiencing longer dry spells, unseasonable heat waves, and erratic weather that undermines yields, even when inputs like fertilizer and water improve.

The study modeled future crop yields under a range of warming and adaptation scenarios. By 2100, the authors estimate global crop yields would be dragged down 11% if emissions rapidly plummet to net zero and 24% if emissions continue to rise unchecked.

In the shorter term, by 2050 the authors estimate climate change will drag global crop yields down by 8% – regardless of how much emissions rise or fall in the coming decades. That’s because carbon dioxide emissions stay in the atmosphere, trapping heat and causing damage for hundreds of years. “If we ignore those long-run damages, we assign an economic value of zero to them, and that is clearly wrong,” Hultgren said.

## New tools aim to help policymakers target resources

Hsiang, Hultgren, and colleagues are now working to help governments make informed decisions about where to direct adaptation investments, recognizing that many farmers still lack access to even basic agricultural resources, such as better fertilizer and accurate weather data.

The team is [working with the United Nations Development Program](https://horizons.hdr.undp.org/ "(opens in a new window)") to disseminate the new climate risk insights to governments around the world and [developing a system](https://sustainability-accelerator.stanford.edu/quantifying-climate-vulnerability-and-adaptation-potential-decision-making-using-satellite-imagery "(opens in a new window)") to identify communities most at risk of yield declines and where targeted support can be most effective. “We’re focusing on how to make it so that this is not actually what our future looks like, even if we can’t get our act together on the emissions side,” Hsiang said.

A favorable climate, he added, is a big part of what keeps farmland productive across generations. “Farmers know how to maintain the soil, invest in infrastructure, repair the barn,” Hsiang said. “But if you’re letting the climate depreciate, the rest of it is a waste. The land you leave to your kids will be good for something, but not for farming.”

Title: Effects of climate change on agriculture - Wikipedia
[Jump to content](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#bodyContent)

From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia

For contributions of agricultural activities to climate change, see [Greenhouse gas emissions from agriculture](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Greenhouse_gas_emissions_from_agriculture "Greenhouse gas emissions from agriculture").

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Aerial_photograph_of_2019_Toki_River_flood_-_GSI_Saitama_51792_collage_ver.jpg/250px-Aerial_photograph_of_2019_Toki_River_flood_-_GSI_Saitama_51792_collage_ver.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Aerial_photograph_of_2019_Toki_River_flood_-_GSI_Saitama_51792_collage_ver.jpg)

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/NASA_CO2_leaf_area_1982-2015_collage_ver.png/250px-NASA_CO2_leaf_area_1982-2015_collage_ver.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:NASA_CO2_leaf_area_1982-2015_collage_ver.png)

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/NASA_Horn_Of_Africa_drought_2022_collage_ver.png/250px-NASA_Horn_Of_Africa_drought_2022_collage_ver.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:NASA_Horn_Of_Africa_drought_2022_collage_ver.png)

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Lagarta_do_Cartucho_03_collage_ver.jpg/250px-Lagarta_do_Cartucho_03_collage_ver.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Lagarta_do_Cartucho_03_collage_ver.jpg)

Examples of the effects of climate change on agriculture: 2019 flooding of the [Toki River](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Toki_River "Toki River") caused by [Typhoon Hagibis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Typhoon_Hagibis "Typhoon Hagibis"), which was exacerbated by [climate change](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change "Climate change");[\[1\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-1) increase in global leaf area primarily caused by the [CO2 fertilization effect](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/CO2_fertilization_effect "CO2 fertilization effect");[\[2\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-2) [2020–2023 Horn of Africa drought](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/2020%E2%80%932023_Horn_of_Africa_drought "2020–2023 Horn of Africa drought"), the worst drought on record and made worse due to the [effects of climate change on the water cycle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_the_water_cycle "Effects of climate change on the water cycle");[\[3\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-WWA2023-3) [maize](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Maize "Maize") plant in [Brazil](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Brazil "Brazil") attacked by [fall armyworm](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fall_armyworm "Fall armyworm"), a [pest](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pest_(organism) "Pest (organism)") that is expected to benefit from climate change.[\[4\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Zacarias2020-4)

There are numerous **effects of [climate change](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change "Climate change") on agriculture**, many of which are making it harder for agricultural activities to provide global [food security](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Food_security "Food security"). Recent research has found that climate change is likely to exacerbate the existing environmental impacts of agriculture by lowering crop productivity, reducing the effectiveness of agrochemicals, increasing soil erosion and pest pressures, and thereby driving greater use of land, water and inputs.[\[5\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-5) Rising temperatures and changing weather patterns often result in lower crop yields due to [water scarcity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Water_scarcity "Water scarcity") caused by [drought](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Drought "Drought"), [heat waves](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Heat_wave "Heat wave") and [flooding](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Flood "Flood").[\[6\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-AR6_WG2_Ch5-6) These [effects of climate change](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change "Effects of climate change") can also increase the risk of several regions suffering simultaneous [crop failures](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Crop_failure "Crop failure").[\[7\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-7) Currently this risk is rare but if these simultaneous crop failures occur, they could have significant consequences for the global food supply.[\[8\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Gaupp2019-8)[\[9\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Kornhuber2023-9) With a growing population and rapid urbanization, it is suggested that food production may increase by nearly 60% in the upcoming years. Verma, Krishnan (2025). ["Climate change adaptation: Challenges for agricultural sustainability"](https://onlinelibrary.wiley.com/doi/full/10.1111/pce.15078). _Plant, Cell & Environment_. **48** (4). Wiley: 2522–2533\. [Bibcode](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bibcode_(identifier) "Bibcode (identifier)"): [2025PCEnv..48.2522V](https://ui.adsabs.harvard.edu/abs/2025PCEnv..48.2522V). [doi](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Doi_(identifier) "Doi (identifier)"): [10.1111/pce.15078](https://doi.org/10.1111%2Fpce.15078). [PMID](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/PMID_(identifier) "PMID (identifier)") [39136256](https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/39136256). Retrieved 30 October 2025.. Many [pests](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pest_(organism) "Pest (organism)") and [plant diseases](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Plant_pathology "Plant pathology") are expected to become more prevalent or to spread to new regions. The world's [livestock](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Livestock "Livestock") are expected to be affected by many of the same issues. These issues range from greater [heat stress](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Heat_stress "Heat stress") to [animal feed](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Animal_feed "Animal feed") shortfalls and the spread of [parasites](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Parasite "Parasite") and [vector-borne diseases](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vector-borne_disease "Vector-borne disease").[\[6\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-AR6_WG2_Ch5-6): 746

The increased [atmospheric CO2 level](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carbon_dioxide_in_Earth%27s_atmosphere "Carbon dioxide in Earth's atmosphere") from human activities (mainly burning of [fossil fuels](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fossil_fuel "Fossil fuel")) causes a [CO2 fertilization effect](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/CO2_fertilization_effect "CO2 fertilization effect"). This effect offsets a small portion of the detrimental effects of climate change on agriculture. However, it comes at the expense of lower levels of essential [micronutrients](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Micronutrient "Micronutrient") in the crops.[\[6\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-AR6_WG2_Ch5-6): 717  Furthermore, CO2 fertilization has little effect on [C4 crops](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/C4_carbon_fixation "C4 carbon fixation") like [maize](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Maize "Maize").[\[10\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Ainsworth2021-10) On the coasts, some agricultural land is expected to be lost to [sea level rise](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sea_level_rise "Sea level rise"), while [melting glaciers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Retreat_of_glaciers_since_1850 "Retreat of glaciers since 1850") could result in less [irrigation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Irrigation "Irrigation") water being available.[\[11\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Biemans2019-11) On the other hand, more [arable land](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arable_land "Arable land") may become available as [frozen land](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Permafrost "Permafrost") thaws. Other effects include erosion and changes in [soil fertility](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soil_fertility "Soil fertility") and the length of growing seasons. Bacteria like _[Salmonella](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Salmonella "Salmonella")_ and [fungi](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fungus "Fungus") that produce [mycotoxins](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mycotoxin "Mycotoxin") grow faster as the climate warms. Their growth has negative effects on [food safety](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Food_safety "Food safety"), [food loss](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Food_loss_and_waste "Food loss and waste") and [prices](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Food_prices "Food prices").[\[6\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-AR6_WG2_Ch5-6)

Extensive research exists on the effects of climate change on individual crops, particularly on the four [staple crops](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Staple_crop "Staple crop"): corn ( [maize](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Maize "Maize")), [rice](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rice "Rice"), [wheat](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wheat "Wheat") and [soybeans](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soybeans "Soybeans"). These crops are responsible for around two-thirds of all calories consumed by humans (both directly and indirectly as animal feed).[\[12\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Zhao2017-12) The research investigates important uncertainties, for example [future population growth](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Future_population_growth "Future population growth"), which will increase global food demand for the foreseeable future.[\[13\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-vanDijk2021-13) The future degree of [soil erosion](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soil_erosion "Soil erosion") and [groundwater depletion](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Groundwater_depletion "Groundwater depletion") are further uncertainties. On the other hand, a range of improvements to agricultural yields, collectively known as the [Green Revolution](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Green_Revolution "Green Revolution"), has increased yields per unit of land area by between 250% and 300% since 1960. Some of that progress will likely continue.[\[6\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-AR6_WG2_Ch5-6): 727

Global food security will change relatively little in the near-term. 720 million to 811 million people were undernourished in 2021, with around 200,000 people being at a _catastrophic level_ of food insecurity.[\[14\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-FAO2021-14) Climate change is expected to add an additional 8 to 80 million people who are at risk of hunger by 2050. The estimated range depends on the intensity of future warming and the effectiveness of [adaptation measures](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change_adaptation "Climate change adaptation").[\[6\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-AR6_WG2_Ch5-6): 717  Agricultural productivity growth will likely have improved food security for hundreds of millions of people by then.[\[15\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Janssens2020-15)[\[13\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-vanDijk2021-13) Predictions that reach further into the future (to 2100 and beyond) are rare. There is some concern about the effects on food security from more [extreme weather](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Extreme_weather "Extreme weather") events in future.[\[16\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Hasegawa2021-16)[\[17\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Schewe2019-17)[\[18\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Kummu2021-18) Nevertheless, at this stage there is no expectation of a widespread global [famine](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Famine "Famine") due to climate change within the 21st century.[\[19\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-WG3_Chapter3-19)[\[20\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Bradshaw2021-20)

## Direct effects from changing weather patterns

\[ [edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture&action=edit&section=1 "Edit section: Direct effects from changing weather patterns")\]

### Observed changes in adverse weather conditions

\[ [edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture&action=edit&section=2 "Edit section: Observed changes in adverse weather conditions")\]

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Br%C3%A1s_2021_EWDs_Europe.jpg/330px-Br%C3%A1s_2021_EWDs_Europe.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Br%C3%A1s_2021_EWDs_Europe.jpg) The observed increase in extreme weather events in [Europe](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Europe "Europe"), from 1964 to 2015.[\[21\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Br%C3%A1s2021-21)[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Burroughs_2022_soybean_temp_curves.jpeg/250px-Burroughs_2022_soybean_temp_curves.jpeg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Burroughs_2022_soybean_temp_curves.jpeg) Soybean plants grow less and develop a smaller leaf area as they are exposed to temperatures beyond what they are historically used to.[\[22\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Burroughs2022-22)

Agriculture is sensitive to weather, and major events like [heatwaves](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Heatwave "Heatwave") or [droughts](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Drought "Drought") or heavy rains (also known as low and high [precipitation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Precipitation "Precipitation") extremes) can cause substantial losses. For example, [Australia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Australia "Australia")'s farmers are very likely to suffer losses during the [El Nino](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/El_Nino "El Nino") weather conditions, while [2003 European heat wave](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/2003_European_heat_wave "2003 European heat wave") led to 13 billion [euros](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Euro "Euro") in uninsured agriculture losses.[\[23\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Tubiello_&_Soussana-23) Climate change is known to increase the frequency and severity of [heatwaves](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Heatwave "Heatwave"), and to make precipitation less predictable and more prone to extremes, but since [climate change attribution](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change_attribution "Climate change attribution") is still a relatively new field, connecting specific weather events and the shortfalls they cause to climate change over natural variability is often difficult. Exceptions include [West Africa](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/West_Africa "West Africa"), where the climate-induced intensification of [extreme weather](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Extreme_weather "Extreme weather") was found to have already decreased [millet](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Millet "Millet") yields by 10–20%, and [sorghum](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sorghum "Sorghum") yields 5–15%. Similarly, it was found that climate change had intensified drought conditions in [Southern Africa](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Southern_Africa "Southern Africa") in 2007, which elevated [food prices](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Food_price "Food price") and caused "acute food insecurity" in the country of [Lesotho](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lesotho "Lesotho"). Agriculture in Southern Africa was also adversely affected by drought after climate change intensified the effects of [2014–2016 El Niño event](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/2014%E2%80%932016_El_Ni%C3%B1o_event "2014–2016 El Niño event").[\[6\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-AR6_WG2_Ch5-6): 724

In [Europe](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Europe "Europe"), between 1950 and 2019, heat extremes became more frequent and more likely to occur consecutively, while cold extremes have declined. At the same time, [Northern Europe](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Northern_Europe "Northern Europe") and much of [Eastern Europe](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Eastern_Europe "Eastern Europe") was found to experience extreme [precipitation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Precipitation "Precipitation") more often, while the [Mediterranean](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mediterranean "Mediterranean") became more affected by [drought](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Drought "Drought").[\[24\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Pradhan2022-24) Similarly, the severity of heatwave and drought effects on European crop production was found to have tripled over a 50-year period – from losses of 2.2% during 1964–1990 to losses of 7.3% in 1991–2015.[\[25\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-:4-25)[\[21\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Br%C3%A1s2021-21) In the summer of 2018, heat waves probably linked to climate change greatly reduced average yield in many parts of the world, especially Europe. During the month of August, more crop failures resulted in a rise in global [food prices](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Food_prices "Food prices").[\[26\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-This_Summer's_Heat_Waves_Could_Be_the_Strongest_Climate_Signal_Yet-26)

On the other hand, floods, often linked to climate change, have also had notable adverse effects on agriculture in the recent years. In May 2019, floods shortened [corn](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Maize "Maize") planting season in the [Midwestern United States](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Midwestern_United_States "Midwestern United States"), lowering the projected yield from 15 billion [bushels](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bushel "Bushel") to 14.2.[\[27\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-27) During the [2021 European floods](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/2021_European_floods "2021 European floods"), estimates pointed to severe damage to the agricultural sector of Belgium, one of the countries hardest hit by the floods, including long-term effects like [soil erosion](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soil_erosion "Soil erosion").[\[28\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-btfarms-28) In [China](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/China "China"), 2023 research found that extreme rainfall had cost the country about 8% of its rice output over the two preceding decades. This was considered comparable to losses caused by extreme heat over this period.[\[29\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Fu2023-29)

### Projected effects from temperature increase

\[ [edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture&action=edit&section=3 "Edit section: Projected effects from temperature increase")\]

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Projected_changes_in_crop_yields_at_different_latitudes_with_global_warming.png/250px-Projected_changes_in_crop_yields_at_different_latitudes_with_global_warming.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Projected_changes_in_crop_yields_at_different_latitudes_with_global_warming.png) Climate-driven changes in crop yields at different latitudes, as projected by the US National Research Council in 2011.[\[30\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-:6-30): Figure 5.1 [![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Corn_and_soybean_temperature_response_%28ARS_USDA%29.png/250px-Corn_and_soybean_temperature_response_%28ARS_USDA%29.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Corn_and_soybean_temperature_response_(ARS_USDA).png) [Maize](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Maize "Maize") will fail to reproduce at temperatures above 35 °C (95 °F) and [soybean](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soybean "Soybean") above 38.8 °C (101.8 °F).[\[31\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-31)

Changes in temperature and weather patterns will alter areas suitable for farming.[\[32\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Connor-32) The current prediction is that temperatures will increase and precipitation will decrease in [arid](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arid "Arid") and [semi-arid](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Semi-arid_climate "Semi-arid climate") regions ( [Middle East](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Middle_East "Middle East"), [Africa](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Africa "Africa"), [Australia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Australia "Australia"), [Southwest United States](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Southwest_United_States "Southwest United States"), and [Southern Europe](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Southern_Europe "Southern Europe")).[\[32\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Connor-32)[\[33\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Tubiello-33) In addition, crop yields in [tropical regions](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tropical_regions "Tropical regions") will be negatively affected by the projected moderate increase in temperature (1–2 °C) expected to occur during the first half of the century.[\[23\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Tubiello_&_Soussana-23) During the second half of the century, further warming is projected to decrease [crop](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Crop "Crop") yields in all regions including [Canada](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Canada "Canada") and the [Northern United States](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Northern_United_States "Northern United States").[\[33\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Tubiello-33) Many [staple crops](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Staple_crop "Staple crop") are extremely sensitive to heat and when temperatures rise over 36 °C (97 °F), soybean seedlings are killed and corn pollen loses its vitality.[\[34\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Ferber_&_Epstein-34)[\[35\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Thomson-35) Warmer temperatures also contribute to the spread and growth of pests and diseases. According to some estimates, a 1 °C increase in global temperature could result in a 10–25% decline in crop yields in the future due to pest growth.[\[36\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-36) According to the Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change (IPCC) 2023 Synthesis Report, global surface temperature has increased by approximately 1.1 °C above the 1850–1900 average.[\[37\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-37)

Higher winter temperatures and more frost-free days in some regions can currently be disruptive, as they can cause [phenological mismatch](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Phenological_mismatch "Phenological mismatch") between flowering time of plants and the activity of [pollinators](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pollinator "Pollinator"), threatening their reproductive success.[\[38\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-38) In the longer term, however, they would result in longer [growing seasons](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Growing_season "Growing season").[\[39\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Kul2-39)[\[40\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Canada-40) For example, a 2014 study found that maize yields in the [Heilongjiang](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Heilongjiang "Heilongjiang") region of China increased by between 7 and 17% per decade as a result of rising temperatures.[\[41\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-41) On the other hand, a year 2017 [meta-analysis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Meta-analysis "Meta-analysis") comparing data from four different methods of estimating effect of warming (two types of climate model, [statistical regressions](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Statistical_regression "Statistical regression") and [field experiments](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Field_experiment "Field experiment") where land around certain crops was warmed by a certain amount to compare them with the controls) concluded that on a global scale, warming alone has consistently negative effects on the yields of four most important crops, suggesting that any increases would be down to [precipitation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Precipitation "Precipitation") changes and the [CO2 fertilization effect](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/CO2_fertilization_effect "CO2 fertilization effect").[\[12\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Zhao2017-12) A 2025 systematic review found that, without effective adaptation measures, climate change could reduce global agricultural food production by up to 14% by 2050.[\[42\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Farah2025-42)

#### Heat stress in livestock

\[ [edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture&action=edit&section=4 "Edit section: Heat stress in livestock")\]

This section is an excerpt from [Effects of climate change on livestock § Projected worldwide increases](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_livestock#Projected_worldwide_increases "Effects of climate change on livestock").\[ [edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Effects_of_climate_change_on_livestock&action=edit)\]

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Lallo_2018_Jamaica_livestock_THI.png/250px-Lallo_2018_Jamaica_livestock_THI.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Lallo_2018_Jamaica_livestock_THI.png) Increased intensity of global climate change causes even greater increases of thermal heat index in Jamaican farm animals. High thermal heat index is one of the more widely used indicators of heat stress.[\[43\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Effects_of_climate_change_on_livestock_Lallo2018-43)

In general, the preferred ambient temperature range for domestic animals is between 10 and 30 °C (50 and 86 °F).[\[44\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Effects_of_climate_change_on_livestock_AR6_WGII_Chapter5-44): 747  Much like how climate change is expected to increase overall thermal comfort for humans living in the colder regions of the world,[\[45\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Effects_of_climate_change_on_livestock_Zhang2023-45) livestock in those places would also benefit from warmer winters.[\[46\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Effects_of_climate_change_on_livestock_Lacetera2018-46) Across the entire world, however, increasing summertime temperatures as well as more frequent and intense heatwaves will have clearly negative effects, substantially elevating the risk of livestock suffering from [heat stress](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Heat_stress "Heat stress"). Under the [climate change scenario](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change_scenario "Climate change scenario") of highest emissions and greatest warming, [SSP5-8.5](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shared_Socioeconomic_Pathways "Shared Socioeconomic Pathways"), "cattle,sheep, goats, pigs and poultry in the low latitudes will face 72–136 additional days per year of extreme stress from high heat and humidity".[\[44\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Effects_of_climate_change_on_livestock_AR6_WGII_Chapter5-44): 717

In [Jamaica](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jamaica "Jamaica"), considered representative of the [Caribbean](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Caribbean "Caribbean") region, all livestock animals besides layer hens are already exposed to "very severe" heat stress in the present climate, with pigs being exposed to it at least once per day during the 5 summer and early autumn months, while [ruminants](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ruminant "Ruminant") and [broilers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Broiler "Broiler") only _avoid_ daily exposure to very severe heat stress during the winter. it has been projected that even at 1.5 °C (2.7 °F) of global warming, "very severe" heat stress would become a _daily_ event for [ruminants](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ruminant "Ruminant") and [broilers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Broiler "Broiler"). By 2 °C (3.6 °F), it would be felt for a longer duration, and extensive cooling systems would likely become a necessity for livestock production in the Caribbean. At 2.5 °C (4.5 °F), only layer hens would avoid daily exposure to "very severe" heat stress during the winter months.[\[43\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Effects_of_climate_change_on_livestock_Lallo2018-43)

Studies of heat stress and livestock had historically focused on cattle, as they are often kept outdoors and so are immediately exposed to changes in climate. On the other hand, a little over 50% of all pork production and 70% of all poultry production worldwide originated from animals kept entirely in confined buildings even around 2006, and the raw numbers were expected to increase by 3–3.5 times for pigs, by 2–2.4 times for [layer hens](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Layer_hen "Layer hen") and 4.4–5 times for [broilers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Broiler "Broiler"). Historically, livestock in these conditions were considered less vulnerable to warming than the animals in outdoor areas due to inhabiting insulated buildings, where [ventilation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ventilation_(architecture) "Ventilation (architecture)") systems are used to control the climate and relieve the excess heat. However, in the historically cooler [midlatitude](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Midlatitude "Midlatitude") regions, indoor temperatures were already higher than the outdoor temperatures even in summer, and as the increased heating exceeds these systems' specifications, confined animals are left more vulnerable to the heat than those kept outdoors.[\[47\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Effects_of_climate_change_on_livestock_Mikovits2019-47)

### Changes in agricultural water availability and reliability

\[ [edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture&action=edit&section=5 "Edit section: Changes in agricultural water availability and reliability")\]

Main article: [Effects of climate change on the water cycle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_the_water_cycle "Effects of climate change on the water cycle")

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Ficklin_2022_SSP45_ET_rainfall.jpg/250px-Ficklin_2022_SSP45_ET_rainfall.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Ficklin_2022_SSP45_ET_rainfall.jpg) Future warming is expected to consistently increase the strength of heavy rainfall, yet it also consistently increases the amount of water plants lose through evapotranspiration.[\[48\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Ficklin2022-48) This effect is counteracted by the CO2 fertilization effect, but at times not enough to avoid events like the [2020–2023 Horn of Africa drought](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/2020%E2%80%932023_Horn_of_Africa_drought "2020–2023 Horn of Africa drought").[\[3\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-WWA2023-3)[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Historical_drought_frequency_on_rainfed_cropland%2C_1984%E2%80%932023.svg/250px-Historical_drought_frequency_on_rainfed_cropland%2C_1984%E2%80%932023.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Historical_drought_frequency_on_rainfed_cropland,_1984%E2%80%932023.svg) Historical drought frequency on rainfed cropland, 1984–2023.

Both [droughts](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Droughts "Droughts") and [floods](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Floods "Floods") contribute to decreases in [crop yields](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Crop_yield "Crop yield").
