On average, climate change increases the overall amount of [water contained in the atmosphere](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Water_vapour "Water vapour") by 7% per every 1 °C (1.8 °F), thus increasing [precipitation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Precipitation "Precipitation").[\[49\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-49)[\[50\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-50) However, this increase in precipitation is not distributed evenly in space ( [atmospheric circulation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Atmospheric_circulation "Atmospheric circulation") patterns already cause different areas to receive different amounts of rainfall) or time: heavy rainfall, with the potential to cause floods, becomes more frequent. This means that under the probable mid-range [climate change scenario](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change_scenario "Climate change scenario"),[\[51\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Schuur2022-51)[\[52\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Phiddian2022-52) SSP2-4.5, precipitation events globally will become larger by 11.5%, yet the time between them will increase by an average of 5.1%. Under the highest-emission scenario [SSP5-8.5](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shared_Socioeconomic_Pathways "Shared Socioeconomic Pathways"), there will be an 18.5% increase in size of events and 9.6% increase in the duration between them. At the same time, water losses by plants through [evapotranspiration](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evapotranspiration "Evapotranspiration") will increase almost everywhere due to higher temperatures.[\[48\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Ficklin2022-48) While the CO2 fertilization effect also reduces such losses by plants, it depends on the area's climate which effect will dominate. As such, the [2020–2023 Horn of Africa drought](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/2020%E2%80%932023_Horn_of_Africa_drought "2020–2023 Horn of Africa drought") has been primarily attributed to the great increase in evapotranspiration exacerbating the effect of persistent low rainfall, which would have been more manageable in the cooler preindustrial climate.[\[3\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-WWA2023-3)

In total, this means that [droughts](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Drought "Drought") have been occurring more frequently on average because of climate change. Africa, southern Europe, the Middle East, most of the Americas, Australia, South and Southeast Asia are the parts of the globe where droughts are expected to become more frequent and intense in spite of the global increase in precipitation.[\[53\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-53) Droughts disturb terrestrial precipitation, evaporation and soil moisture,[\[54\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-54)[\[55\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-55) and these effects can be aggravated by [population growth](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Population_growth "Population growth") and [urban expansion](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Urban_expansion "Urban expansion") spurring on increased demand for water.[\[56\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-56) The ultimate outcome is [water scarcity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Water_scarcity "Water scarcity"), which results in crop failures and the loss of pasture grazing land for livestock,[\[57\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-57) exacerbating pre-existing [poverty](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Poverty "Poverty") in [developing countries](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Developing_country "Developing country") and leading to [malnutrition](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Malnutrition "Malnutrition") and potentially [famine](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Famine "Famine").[\[58\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Hertel2-58)[\[34\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Ferber_&_Epstein-34)

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/HKH-Glacier-Mass-Change.png/250px-HKH-Glacier-Mass-Change.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:HKH-Glacier-Mass-Change.png) Observed glacier mass loss in the [Hindu Kush](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hindu_Kush "Hindu Kush") [Himalayas](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Himalayas "Himalayas") region since the 20th century.[\[59\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-:HKH2019Cryo-59)

[Irrigation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Irrigation "Irrigation") of crops is able to reduce or even remove the effects on yields of lower rainfall and higher temperatures – through localized cooling. However, using water resources for irrigation has downsides and is expensive.[\[32\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Connor-32) Further, some sources of irrigation water may become less reliable. This includes irrigation driven by [water runoff](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Surface_runoff "Surface runoff") from [glaciers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glaciers "Glaciers") during the summer, as there has already been an observed [retreat of glaciers since 1850](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Retreat_of_glaciers_since_1850 "Retreat of glaciers since 1850"), and it is expected to continue, depleting the glacial ice and reducing or outright eliminating runoff.[\[60\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-60) In Asia, global warming of 1.5 °C (2.7 °F) will reduce the ice mass of Asia's high mountains by about 29–43%,:[\[61\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-61) Approximately 2.4 billion people live in the [drainage basin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Drainage_basin "Drainage basin") of the Himalayan rivers:[\[62\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-62) In [India](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/India "India") alone, the river [Ganges](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ganges "Ganges") provides water for drinking and farming for more than 500 million people.[\[63\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-63)[\[64\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-64) In the [Indus River](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Indus_River "Indus River") watershed, these mountain water resources contribute to up to 60% of irrigation outside of the monsoon season, and an additional 11% of total crop production.[\[11\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Biemans2019-11) Since [Effects of climate change on the water cycle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_the_water_cycle "Effects of climate change on the water cycle") are projected to substantially increase precipitation in all but the westernmost parts of the watershed, the loss of the glaciers is expected to be offset: however, agriculture in the region will become more reliable on monsoon than ever, and [hydropower](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hydropower "Hydropower") generation would become less predictable and reliable.[\[65\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-:HKH2019Climate-65)[\[59\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-:HKH2019Cryo-59)[\[66\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-:HKH2019Water-66)

## Effects on plants caused by increasing atmospheric CO2 and methane

\[ [edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture&action=edit&section=6 "Edit section: Effects on plants caused by increasing atmospheric CO2 and methane")\]

Plants respond to increased atmospheric carbon dioxide concentrations through multiple physiological changes. Atmospheric [carbon dioxide](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carbon_dioxide "Carbon dioxide") (CO2) increases affect plant growth patterns and physiological functions in multiple ways. Higher levels of carbon dioxide amplify both crop yields and biomass production through enhanced [photosynthetic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Photosynthesis "Photosynthesis") processes. The partial closure of [stomata](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stoma "Stoma") under elevated CO2 levels leads to reduced plant water loss and enhances water-use efficiency.[\[67\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-67) Elevated atmospheric CO2 and CH4 concentrations can have a range of effects on plant growth, physiology and ecosystem process via multiple pathways. In the case of C3 plants, CO2 fertilization usually more than doubles the initial stimulation of photosynthesis by increased CO2, resulting in increased rates of carbon assimilation, vegetative growth, and, under some circumstances, crop yield. This so-called CO2 fertilization effect has been demonstrated in controlled systems, such as Free-Air CO2 Enrichment (FACE) trials that are designed to mimic predicted future atmospheric conditions and monitor plant responses under open-air conditions.[\[68\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-68)[\[69\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-69) Moreover, increased CO2 can cause partial stomatal closing to reduce transpiration, and enhance water use-efficiency in plant, especially under drought stress.[\[70\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-70)

However, the benefits of CO2 enrichment may be moderated by nutrient limitations, particularly nitrogen and phosphorus, which constrain biomass accumulation in natural ecosystems.[\[71\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-71) Methane, while less directly involved in photosynthesis, contributes to climate warming and alters plant communities indirectly through changes in temperature, precipitation patterns, and soil conditions. In wetland environments, increased atmospheric methane can enhance methanotrophic activity in the rhizosphere, influencing nutrient cycling and root-microbe interactions.[\[72\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-72) Furthermore, elevated greenhouse gas levels may affect plant-insect interactions, pathogen dynamics, and secondary metabolite production, complicating predictions about future ecosystem health and productivity.[\[73\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-73)

### Higher yields due to CO2 fertilization

\[ [edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture&action=edit&section=7 "Edit section: Higher yields due to CO2 fertilization")\]

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Projected_effect_of_climate_change_on_agricultural_productivity_for_different_regions_%28Cline%2C_2007%29.png/250px-Projected_effect_of_climate_change_on_agricultural_productivity_for_different_regions_%28Cline%2C_2007%29.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Projected_effect_of_climate_change_on_agricultural_productivity_for_different_regions_(Cline,_2007).png) CO2 fertilization (grey) results in substantially less severe cumulative effects on agricultural productivity compared to its absence (black), as estimated by the IPCC [in 2007](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/IPCC_Fourth_Assessment_Report "IPCC Fourth Assessment Report").[\[74\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Cline2008-74)

This section is an excerpt from [CO2 fertilization effect § Observations and trends](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/CO2_fertilization_effect#Observations_and_trends "CO2 fertilization effect").\[ [edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=CO2_fertilization_effect&action=edit)\]

A 1993 review of scientific greenhouse studies found that a doubling of CO2 concentration would stimulate the growth of 156 different plant species by an average of 37%. Response varied significantly by species, with some showing much greater gains and a few showing a loss. For example, a 1979 greenhouse study found that with doubled CO2 concentration the dry weight of 40-day-old cotton plants doubled, but the dry weight of 30-day-old maize plants increased by only 20%.[\[75\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-75)[\[76\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-76)

In addition to greenhouse studies, field and satellite measurements attempt to understand the effect of increased CO2 in more natural environments. In [free-air carbon dioxide enrichment](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Free-air_concentration_enrichment "Free-air concentration enrichment") (FACE) experiments plants are grown in field plots and the CO2 concentration of the surrounding air is artificially elevated. These experiments generally use lower CO2 levels than the greenhouse studies. They show lower gains in growth than greenhouse studies, with the gains depending heavily on the species under study. A 2005 review of 12 experiments at 475–600 ppm showed an average gain of 17% in [crop yield](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Crop_yield "Crop yield"), with [legumes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Legumes "Legumes") typically showing a greater response than other species and [C4 plants](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/C4_carbon_fixation "C4 carbon fixation") generally showing less. The review also stated that the experiments have their own limitations. The studied CO2 levels were lower, and most of the experiments were carried out in temperate regions.[\[77\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-77) Satellite measurements found increasing [leaf area index](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Leaf_area_index "Leaf area index") for 25% to 50% of Earth's vegetated area over the past 35 years (i.e., a greening of the planet), providing evidence for a positive CO2 fertilization effect.[\[78\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-CO2_fertilization_effect_Zhu_2016-78)[\[79\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-79)

Although the greening of vegetated parts of Earth has been noted, the continuing positive effect of CO2 fertilization will remain uncertain over the long-term. Historically, agriculture has relied on the use of synthetic fertilizers, herbicides, and insecticides, combined with advancements in irrigation and biotechnological approaches to boost productivity. Verma, Krishnan (2025). ["Climate change adaptation: Challenges for agricultural sustainability"](https://onlinelibrary.wiley.com/doi/full/10.1111/pce.15078). _Plant, Cell & Environment_. **48** (4). Wiley: 2522–2533\. [Bibcode](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bibcode_(identifier) "Bibcode (identifier)"): [2025PCEnv..48.2522V](https://ui.adsabs.harvard.edu/abs/2025PCEnv..48.2522V). [doi](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Doi_(identifier) "Doi (identifier)"): [10.1111/pce.15078](https://doi.org/10.1111%2Fpce.15078). [PMID](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/PMID_(identifier) "PMID (identifier)") [39136256](https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/39136256). Retrieved 30 October 2025.. It has been reported that the positive effects of elevated CO2 on plant growth may be attenuated by nutrient limitations, particularly N and P, which are crucial for biomass production and long-term productivity.[\[80\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-leakey2019-80)[\[81\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-terrer2021-81) Moreover, although satellite data indicate a widespread increase in LAI, the greening trend does not necessarily translate into a proportionate increase in carbon sequestration, because of reduced carbon residence times and enhanced ecosystem respiration with warming, as well as unaccounted carbon losses.[\[82\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-zhu2016-82)[\[83\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-piao2010-83) Thus, despite enhancing global greening through CO2 fertilization, the potential for it to compensate for anthropogenic emissions appears to be restricted without corresponding adjustments to nutrient supply and land management.[\[84\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-reich2014-84)

### Reduced nutritional value of crops

\[ [edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture&action=edit&section=8 "Edit section: Reduced nutritional value of crops")\]

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Loladze_2014_micronutrients.jpg/250px-Loladze_2014_micronutrients.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Loladze_2014_micronutrients.jpg) Average decrease of [micronutrient](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Micronutrient "Micronutrient") density across a range of crops at elevated CO2 concentrations, reconstructed from multiple studies through a [meta-analysis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Meta-analysis "Meta-analysis").[\[85\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Loladze2014-85) The elevated concentration in this figure, 689 ppm, is over 50% greater than the current levels, yet it is expected to be approached under the "mid-range" climate change scenarios, and will be surpassed in the high-emission one.[\[86\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Riahi2017-86)

Changes in atmospheric [carbon dioxide](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carbon_dioxide "Carbon dioxide") may reduce the [nutritional](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nutrition "Nutrition") quality of some crops, with for instance wheat having less protein and less of some minerals.[\[87\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Mbow_2019-87): 439 [\[88\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-science-news-88) The nutritional quality of [C3 plants](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/C3_carbon_fixation "C3 carbon fixation") (e.g. wheat, oats, rice) is especially at risk: lower levels of protein as well as minerals (for example zinc and iron) are expected.[\[6\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-AR6_WG2_Ch5-6): 1379  Food crops could see a reduction of [protein](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Protein "Protein"), [iron](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iron "Iron") and [zinc](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zinc "Zinc") content in common food crops of 3 to 17%.[\[89\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-89) This is the projected result of food grown under the expected atmospheric carbon-dioxide levels of 2050. Using data from the [UN Food and Agriculture Organization](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Food_and_Agriculture_Organization "Food and Agriculture Organization") as well as other public sources, the authors analysed 225 different staple foods, such as [wheat](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wheat "Wheat"), [rice](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rice "Rice"), [maize](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Maize "Maize"), [vegetables](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vegetable "Vegetable"), roots and [fruits](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fruit "Fruit").[\[90\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-90)

The effect of increased levels of atmospheric carbon dioxide on the nutritional quality of plants is not limited only to the above-mentioned crop categories and nutrients. A 2014 [meta-analysis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Meta-analysis "Meta-analysis") has shown that crops and wild plants exposed to elevated [carbon dioxide](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carbon_dioxide "Carbon dioxide") levels at various latitudes have lower density of several minerals such as magnesium, iron, zinc, and potassium.[\[85\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Loladze2014-85)

Studies using [Free-Air Concentration Enrichment](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Free-Air_Concentration_Enrichment "Free-Air Concentration Enrichment") have also shown that increases in CO2 lead to decreased concentrations of [micronutrients](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Micronutrient "Micronutrient") in crop and non-crop plants with negative consequences for human nutrition,[\[91\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-91)[\[85\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Loladze2014-85) including decreased B vitamins in rice.[\[92\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Zhu2018-92)[\[93\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-93) This may have knock-on effects on other parts of [ecosystems](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ecosystem "Ecosystem") as herbivores will need to eat more food to gain the same amount of protein.[\[94\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-94)

A 2022 narrative review highlighted that climate change not only impacts food quantity but also reduces the nutritional quality of crops. Elevated atmospheric CO2 levels have been found to lower concentrations of protein, iron, zinc, and other micronutrients in major staple foods such as wheat and rice. These nutrient losses can lead to widespread deficiencies, particularly in vulnerable populations, and contribute to increased risks of malnutrition. The authors emphasize that climate-smart agriculture and sustainable food systems are urgently needed to protect both food security and human health in the face of these challenges.[\[95\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-95)

Empirical evidence shows that increasing levels of CO2 result in lower concentrations of many minerals in plant tissues. Doubling CO2 levels results in an 8% decline, on average, in the concentration of minerals.[\[85\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Loladze2014-85) Declines in magnesium, calcium, potassium, iron, zinc and other minerals in crops can worsen the quality of human nutrition. Researchers report that the CO2 levels expected in the second half of the 21st century will likely reduce the levels of zinc, iron, and protein in wheat, rice, peas, and soybeans. Some two billion people live in countries where citizens receive more than 60 per cent of their zinc or iron from these types of crops. Deficiencies of these nutrients already cause an estimated loss of 63 million life-years annually.[\[96\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-96)[\[97\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-97)

Alongside a decrease in minerals, evidence shows that plants contain 6% more carbon, 15% less nitrogen, 9% less phosphorus, and 9% less sulfur at double CO2 conditions. The increase in carbon is mostly attributed to [carbohydrates](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carbohydrates "Carbohydrates") without a structural role in plants – the human-digestable, calorie-providing starch and simple sugars. The decrease in nitrogen translates directly into a decrease in the protein content. As a result, higher CO2 not only reduce a plant's micronutrients, but also the quality of its macronutrient combination.

These nutritional declines have severe consequences for public health worldwide, and especially in areas where they have traditionally consumed plant-based diets. Populations in low- and middle-income countries are particularly at risk, having staple crops like rice and wheat as the primary sources of their daily protein, iron and zinc.[\[98\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-smith2018-98) Potential nutrient losses as a result of changes in crop composition due to CO2 could make any deficiencies that already exist worse and increase the incidence of anemia, stunting, and the development of cognitive skills.[\[99\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-myers2014-99) Apart from the effect on humans, such changes in nutrient content can cascade through food webs, with herbivores needing to ingest more biomass to meet their dietary needs, which could have implications for ecosystem dynamics.[\[100\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-loladze2014-100) These results highlight the importance of not just looking at crop yields in future climate scenarios, but also at the nutritional merits of yields in terms of their potential to support human and ecological health.[\[101\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-long2015-101)

### Increasing damages from surface-level ozone

\[ [edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture&action=edit&section=9 "Edit section: Increasing damages from surface-level ozone")\]

Anthropogenic [methane emissions](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Methane_emissions "Methane emissions") have a significant contribution to warming due to the high [global warming potential](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Global_warming_potential "Global warming potential") of methane. At the same time, methane also acts as a precursor to [surface ozone](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ground_level_ozone "Ground level ozone"), which is a significant [air pollutant](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Air_pollution "Air pollution"). Its effects include lowering [physiological functions](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Physiological_functional_capacity "Physiological functional capacity") and therefore the yield and quality of crops.[\[6\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-AR6_WG2_Ch5-6): 732  Following methane levels, tropospheric ozone levels "increased substantially since the late 19th century",[\[6\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-AR6_WG2_Ch5-6): 732  and according to a 2016 estimate, the four major crops (see later section) experienced yield losses of 5±1.5% relative to a no-climate change scenario due to ozone increases alone, which is nearly half of the negative effects caused by the other effects of climate change (10.9±3.2%), and cancels out most of the CO2 fertilization effect (6.5±1.0%).[\[6\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-AR6_WG2_Ch5-6): 724

## Changes in the extent and quality of agricultural land

\[ [edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture&action=edit&section=10 "Edit section: Changes in the extent and quality of agricultural land")\]

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Jasechko_2020_USA_coastal_groundwater.png/250px-Jasechko_2020_USA_coastal_groundwater.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Jasechko_2020_USA_coastal_groundwater.png) Some locations alongside the [United States](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_States "United States")' coast have over half of their freshwater wells below sea level, leaving them vulnerable to saltwater infiltration.[\[102\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Jasechko2020-102)

### Erosion and soil fertility

\[ [edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture&action=edit&section=11 "Edit section: Erosion and soil fertility")\]

The warmer atmospheric temperatures observed over the past decades are expected to lead to a more vigorous hydrological cycle, including more extreme rainfall events. [Erosion](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Erosion "Erosion"), [soil degradation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soil_degradation "Soil degradation"), and [soil compaction](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soil_compaction_(agriculture) "Soil compaction (agriculture)") are more likely to occur as a result of heavy rainfall and extreme weather events.[\[103\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-103) [Soil fertility](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soil_fertility "Soil fertility") would also be affected by global warming. These restrict crop development. Increased erosion in agricultural landscapes from anthropogenic factors can occur with losses of up to 22% of soil carbon in 50 years.[\[104\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-104)

Climate change will also cause soils to warm. Elevated temperatures can accelerate the decomposition of soil organic matter, reducing soil organic carbon and decreasing the soil's ability to retain nutrients and hold water.[\[105\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-105)[\[106\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-106) These factors collectively contribute to reduced soil quality, thus affecting crop growth. In turn, this could cause the [soil microbe](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soil_microbiology "Soil microbiology") population size to dramatically increase 40–150%. Warmer conditions would favour growth of certain bacteria species, shifting the bacterial community composition. Elevated carbon dioxide would increase the growth rates of plants and soil microbes, slowing the soil carbon cycle and favouring [oligotrophs](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Oligotroph "Oligotroph"), which are slower-growing and more resource efficient than [copiotrophs](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Copiotroph "Copiotroph").[\[107\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-107)

### Agricultural land loss from sea level rise

\[ [edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture&action=edit&section=12 "Edit section: Agricultural land loss from sea level rise")\]

A [rise in the sea level](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sea_level_rise "Sea level rise") would result in an agricultural [land loss](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Land_consumption "Land consumption"), in particular in areas such as [South East Asia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/South_East_Asia "South East Asia").[\[108\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-108) [Erosion](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Erosion "Erosion"), [submergence of shorelines](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sea_level_rise "Sea level rise"), [salinity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Salinity "Salinity") of the [water table](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Water_table "Water table") due to the increased sea levels, could mainly affect agriculture through [inundation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Inundation "Inundation") of [low-lying lands](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Depression_(geology) "Depression (geology)"). Low-lying areas such as [Bangladesh](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bangladesh "Bangladesh"), [India](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/India "India") and [Vietnam](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vietnam "Vietnam") will experience major loss of rice crop if sea levels rise as expected by the end of the century. Vietnam for example relies heavily on its southern tip, where the [Mekong Delta](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mekong_Delta "Mekong Delta") lies, for rice planting. A one-metre rise in sea level will cover several square kilometres of [rice paddies](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Paddy_field "Paddy field") in Vietnam.[\[109\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-109)

Besides simply flooding agricultural land, sea level rise can also cause [saltwater intrusion](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Saltwater_intrusion "Saltwater intrusion") into freshwater [wells](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Well "Well"), particularly if they are already below sea level. Once the concentration of saltwater exceeds 2–3%, the well becomes unusable. Notably, areas along an estimated 15% of the US coastline already have the majority of local [groundwater](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Groundwater "Groundwater") below the sea level.[\[102\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Jasechko2020-102)

### Thawing of potentially arable land

\[ [edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture&action=edit&section=13 "Edit section: Thawing of potentially arable land")\]

Climate change may increase the amount of [arable land](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arable_land "Arable land") by reducing the amount of frozen land. A 2005 study reports that temperature in Siberia has increased three-degree Celsius in average since 1960 (much more than the rest of the world).[\[110\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-110)\[ _[needs update](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers#Chronological_items "Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers")_\] However, reports about the effect of global warming on Russian agriculture[\[111\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-111) indicate conflicting probable effects: while they expect a northward extension of farmable lands,[\[112\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-112) they also warn of possible productivity losses and increased risk of drought.[\[113\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-113)\[ _[needs update](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers#Chronological_items "Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers")_\]

The [Arctic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arctic "Arctic") region is expected to benefit from increased opportunities for agriculture and [forestry](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Forestry "Forestry").[\[114\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-114)

## Response of insects, plant diseases and weeds

\[ [edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture&action=edit&section=14 "Edit section: Response of insects, plant diseases and weeds")\]

Further information: [Climate change and invasive species](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change_and_invasive_species "Climate change and invasive species") and [Pest (organism) § In agriculture and horticulture](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pest_(organism)#In_agriculture_and_horticulture "Pest (organism)")

Climate change will alter [pest](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pest_(organism) "Pest (organism)"), [plant disease](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Plant_pathology "Plant pathology") and [weed](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Weed "Weed") distributions, with potential to reduce crop yields, including of [staple crops](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Staple_food "Staple food") like [wheat](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wheat "Wheat"), [soybeans](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soybean "Soybean"), and corn (maize).[\[115\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Live_Science-115) Warmer temperatures can increase the [metabolic rate](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Basal_metabolic_rate "Basal metabolic rate") and number of breeding cycles of [insect](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Insect "Insect") populations.[\[115\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Live_Science-115) Historically, [cold temperatures at night](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Diurnal_temperature_range "Diurnal temperature range") and in the [winter](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Winter "Winter") months would kill off [insects](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Insects "Insects"), [bacteria](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bacteria "Bacteria") and [fungi](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fungi "Fungi"). The warmer, wetter winters are promoting fungal plant diseases like wheat rusts ( [stripe](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stripe_rust "Stripe rust") and [brown/leaf](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wheat_brown_rust "Wheat brown rust")) and [soybean rust](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soybean_rust "Soybean rust") to travel northward.[\[116\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Luck-et-al-2011-116) The increasing incidence of flooding and heavy rains also promotes the growth of various other plant pests and diseases.[\[117\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Rod112-117)

### Insect pollinators and pests

\[ [edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture&action=edit&section=15 "Edit section: Insect pollinators and pests")\]

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Swarm_of_Locusts.JPG/250px-Swarm_of_Locusts.JPG)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Swarm_of_Locusts.JPG) Locust swarm near [Satrokala](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Satrokala "Satrokala"), [Madagascar](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Madagascar "Madagascar") in 2014.

Climate change is expected to have a negative effect on many insects, greatly reducing their [species distribution](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Species_distribution "Species distribution") and thus increasing their [risk of going extinct](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Extinction_risk_from_climate_change#Insects "Extinction risk from climate change").[\[118\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Warren2018b-118) Around 9% of agricultural production is dependent in some way on [insect pollination](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Insect_pollination "Insect pollination"),[\[119\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-119) and some [pollinator](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pollinator "Pollinator") species are also adversely affected, with wild [bumblebees](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bumblebee "Bumblebee") known to be particularly vulnerable to recent warming.[\[120\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-120)[\[121\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-121)

At the same time, insects are the most diverse animal taxa, and some species will benefit from the changes, including notable agricultural pests and [disease vectors](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vector_(epidemiology) "Vector (epidemiology)").[\[116\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Luck-et-al-2011-116) Insects that previously had only two breeding cycles per year could gain an additional cycle if warm growing seasons extend, causing a population boom. Temperate places and higher [latitudes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Latitude "Latitude") are more likely to experience a dramatic change in insect populations:[\[122\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-122) for instance, the [Mountain Pine Beetle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mountain_Pine_Beetle "Mountain Pine Beetle") epidemic in [British Columbia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/British_Columbia "British Columbia"), [Canada](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Canada "Canada") had killed millions of pine trees, partially because the winters were not cold enough to slow or kill the growing beetle larvae.[\[34\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Ferber_&_Epstein-34) Likewise, [potato tuber moth](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Potato_tuber_moth "Potato tuber moth") and [Colorado potato beetle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Colorado_potato_beetle "Colorado potato beetle") are predicted to spread into areas currently too cold for them.[\[123\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-supply-123)

Further, [effects of climate change on the water cycle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_the_water_cycle "Effects of climate change on the water cycle") often mean that both wet seasons and drought seasons will become more intense. Some insect species will breed more rapidly because they are better able to take advantage of such changes in conditions.[\[124\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Sgro-et-al-2016-124) This includes certain insect pests, such as [aphids](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Aphids "Aphids") and [whiteflies](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Whiteflies "Whiteflies"):[\[34\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Ferber_&_Epstein-34) similarly, [locust](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Locust "Locust") swarms could also cause more damage as the result. A notable example was the [2019–2022 locust infestation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/2019%E2%80%932022_locust_infestation "2019–2022 locust infestation") focused on [East Africa](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/East_Africa "East Africa"), considered the worst of its kind in many decades.[\[125\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-:7-125)[\[126\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-126)

The [fall armyworm](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fall_armyworm "Fall armyworm"), _Spodoptera frugiperda_, is a highly invasive plant pest, which can cause have massive damage to crops, especially maize. In the recent years, it has spread to countries in [sub-Saharan Africa](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sub-Saharan_Africa "Sub-Saharan Africa"), and this spread is linked to climate change. It is expected that these highly invasive crop pests will spread to other parts of the planet since they have a high capacity to adapt to different environments.[\[4\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Zacarias2020-4)

### Invasive plant species

\[ [edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture&action=edit&section=16 "Edit section: Invasive plant species")\]

|     |     |
| --- | --- |
| [![[icon]](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/20px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg) | **This section is empty.** You can help by [adding to it](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture&action=edit&section=). _(January 2024)_ |

### Weeds

\[ [edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture&action=edit&section=17 "Edit section: Weeds")\]

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Field_visit_to_NEON_research_site_in_Front_Royal%2C_Virginia_%2825533860793%29.jpg/250px-Field_visit_to_NEON_research_site_in_Front_Royal%2C_Virginia_%2825533860793%29.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Field_visit_to_NEON_research_site_in_Front_Royal,_Virginia_(25533860793).jpg) A continental-scale research platform for long-term study of the effects of climate change, land-use change and invasive species on ecological systems (research site in [Front Royal](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Front_Royal,_Virginia "Front Royal, Virginia"), Virginia, U.S.)

A changing climate may favour the more biologically diverse [weeds](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Weed "Weed") over the [monocrops](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Monocropping "Monocropping") on many farms.[\[117\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Rod112-117) Characteristics of weeds such as their [genetic diversity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Genetic_diversity "Genetic diversity"), [cross-breeding](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cross_breeding "Cross breeding") ability, and fast-growth rates put them at an advantage in changing climates as these characteristics allow them to [adapt](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Adaptation "Adaptation") readily in comparison to most farm's uniform crops, and give them a biological advantage.[\[34\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Ferber_&_Epstein-34)

Weeds also undergo the same acceleration of cycles as [cultivated crops](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cultivated_crops "Cultivated crops"), and would also benefit from [CO2 fertilization](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/CO2_fertilization_effect "CO2 fertilization effect"). Since most weeds are [C3 plants](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/C3_carbon_fixation "C3 carbon fixation"), they are likely to compete even more than now against [C4 crops](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/C4_carbon_fixation "C4 carbon fixation") such as corn.[\[127\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-UNL-127) The increased CO2 levels are also expected to increase the tolerance of weeds to [herbicides](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Herbicides "Herbicides"), reducing their efficiency.[\[117\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Rod112-117) However, this may be counteracted by increased temperatures elevating their effectiveness.[\[127\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-UNL-127)

### Plant pathogens

\[ [edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture&action=edit&section=18 "Edit section: Plant pathogens")\]

Currently, pathogens result in losses of 10–16% of the global harvest and this level is likely to rise as plants are at an ever-increasing risk of exposure to pests and [pathogens](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pathogen "Pathogen").[\[128\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Chakra-128) Research has shown that climate change may alter the developmental stages of [plant pathogens](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Plant_pathology#Plant_pathogens "Plant pathology") that can affect crops. This includes several pathogens associated with potato blackleg disease (e.g. [Dickeya](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dickeya "Dickeya")), as they grow and reproduce faster at higher temperatures.[\[129\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-129) The warming is also expected to elevate [food safety](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Food_safety "Food safety") issues and food spoilage caused by [mycotoxin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mycotoxin "Mycotoxin")-producing fungi, and bacteria such as [Salmonella](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Salmonella "Salmonella").[\[130\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-:0-130)

Climate change would cause an increase in rainfall in some areas, which would lead to an increase of atmospheric humidity and the duration of the [wet seasons](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wet_season "Wet season"). Combined with higher temperatures, these conditions could favour the development of [fungal](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fungi "Fungi") diseases, such as [late blight](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Late_blight "Late blight"),[\[131\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-131)[\[116\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Luck-et-al-2011-116) or bacterial infections such as [Ralstonia solanacearum](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ralstonia_solanacearum "Ralstonia solanacearum"), which may also be able to spread more easily through [flash flooding](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Flash_flooding "Flash flooding").[\[123\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-supply-123)

Climate change has the capability of altering pathogen and host interactions, specifically the rates of pathogen infection and the resistance of the host plant.[\[132\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-132) Also affected by plant disease are the economic costs associated with growing different plants that might yield less profit as well as treating and managing already diseased crops.[\[133\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-133) For instance, [soybean rust](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soybean_rust "Soybean rust") is a vicious plant pathogen that can kill off entire fields in a matter of days, devastating farmers and costing billions in agricultural losses.[\[34\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Ferber_&_Epstein-34) Change in weather patterns and temperature due to climate change leads to dispersal of [plant pathogens](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Plant_pathogens "Plant pathogens") as hosts migrate to areas with more favourable conditions. This increases crop losses due to diseases.[\[130\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-:0-130)[\[116\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Luck-et-al-2011-116) For instance, [aphids](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Aphids "Aphids") act as vectors for many potato [viruses](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Virus "Virus") and will be able to spread further due to increased temperatures.[\[134\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-134)

## Effects on crop yields

\[ [edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture&action=edit&section=19 "Edit section: Effects on crop yields")\]

### Observed effects

\[ [edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture&action=edit&section=20 "Edit section: Observed effects")\]

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Ray_2019_climate_crop_map_four_major.png/500px-Ray_2019_climate_crop_map_four_major.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Ray_2019_climate_crop_map_four_major.png) The already observed effects of climate change on the production of four major crops.[\[135\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Ray2019-135)

According to the [IPCC Sixth Assessment Report](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/IPCC_Sixth_Assessment_Report "IPCC Sixth Assessment Report") from 2022, there is _high confidence_ that in and of itself, climate change to date has left primarily negative effects on both [crop yields](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Crop_yield "Crop yield") and quality of produce, although there has been some regional variation:[\[6\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-AR6_WG2_Ch5-6): 724  more negative effects have been observed for some crops in low-latitudes (maize and wheat), while positive effects of climate change have been observed in some crops in high-latitudes (maize, wheat, and [sugar beets](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sugar_beet "Sugar beet")).[\[136\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-136): 8  I.e. during the period 1981 to 2008, global warming has had negative effects on wheat yield in especially tropical regions, with decreases in average global yields by 5.5%.[\[137\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-137) A study in 2019 tracked ~20,000 political units globally for 10 crops ( [maize](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Maize "Maize"), [rice](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rice "Rice"), [wheat](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wheat "Wheat"), [soybean](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soybean "Soybean"), [barley](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barley "Barley"), [cassava](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cassava "Cassava"), [oil palm](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Oil_palm "Oil palm"), [rapeseed](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rapeseed "Rapeseed"), [sorghum](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sorghum "Sorghum") and [sugarcane](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sugarcane "Sugarcane")), providing more detail on the spatial resolution and a larger number of crops than previously studied.[\[135\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Ray2019-135) It found that crop yields across Europe, sub-Saharan Africa, and Australia had in general decreased because of climate change (compared to the baseline value of 2004–2008 average data), though exceptions are present. The effect of global climate change on yields of different crops from climate trends ranged from −13.4% (oil palm) to 3.5% (soybean). The study also showed that effects are generally positive in Latin America. Effects in Asia and Northern and Central America are mixed.[\[135\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Ray2019-135) Recent empirical research finds that each 1°C increase in global mean surface temperature could reduce global staple crop production by about 5.5x10^14 kcal annually (≈120kcal/person/day, or ~4.4% of recommended consumption), even after accounting for adaptation and income growth, with only about one-quarter of losses mitigated by mid-century. (Hultgren et al., 2025)[\[138\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Hultgren2025-138)

While the [Green Revolution](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Green_Revolution "Green Revolution") had ensured the growth of overall crop production per land area of 250% to 300% since the 1960,[\[6\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-AR6_WG2_Ch5-6): 727  with around 44% attributed to newer crop varieties alone,[\[139\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-139) it is believed this growth would have been even greater without the counteracting role of climate change on major crop yields over the same period. Between 1961 and 2021, global agricultural productivity could have been 21% greater than it actually was, if it did not have to contend with climate change. Such shortfalls would have affected [food security](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Food_security "Food security") of [vulnerable populations](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Social_vulnerability "Social vulnerability") the most:[\[6\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-AR6_WG2_Ch5-6): 724  a study in 2019 showed that climate change has already increased the risk of food insecurity in many food insecure countries.[\[135\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Ray2019-135) Even in [developed countries](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Developed_countries "Developed countries") such as [Australia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Australia "Australia"), extreme weather associated with climate change has been found to cause a wide range of cascading spillovers through [supply chain](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Supply_chain "Supply chain") disruption, in addition to its primary effect on fruit, vegetable, and livestock sectors and the rural communities reliant on them.[\[140\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Malik2022-140)

Between 1961 and 1985, [cereal](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cereal "Cereal") production more than doubled in [developing nations](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Developing_nations "Developing nations"), largely due to the development of irrigation, fertilizer, and seed varieties.[\[141\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-conway1-141) Even in the absence of further scientific/technological developments, many of the existing advancements have not been evenly distributed, and their spread from the [developed world](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Developed_world "Developed world") to the [developing world](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Developing_world "Developing world") is expected to drive some improvements on its own. Further, [agricultural expansion](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Agricultural_expansion "Agricultural expansion") has slowed down in the recent years, but this trend is widely expected to reverse in the future in order to maintain the global food supply under all but the most optimistic [climate change scenarios](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change_scenario "Climate change scenario") consistent with the [Paris Agreement](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Paris_Agreement "Paris Agreement").[\[142\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Nelson2013-142)[\[86\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Riahi2017-86)

### Generalized yield projections

\[ [edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture&action=edit&section=21 "Edit section: Generalized yield projections")\]

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Projected_impact_of_climate_change_on_agricultural_yields_by_the_2080s%2C_compared_to_2003_levels_%28Cline%2C_2007%29.png/250px-Projected_impact_of_climate_change_on_agricultural_yields_by_the_2080s%2C_compared_to_2003_levels_%28Cline%2C_2007%29.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Projected_impact_of_climate_change_on_agricultural_yields_by_the_2080s,_compared_to_2003_levels_(Cline,_2007).png) Map of predicted climate change effects on agricultural yields between 2003 and 2080 (data from 2007).[\[74\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Cline2008-74)

In 2007, the [IPCC Fourth Assessment Report](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/IPCC_Fourth_Assessment_Report "IPCC Fourth Assessment Report") had suggested that global production potential would increase up to around 3 °C (5.4 °F) of globally averaged warming, as productivity increases for cereals in high latitudes would outweigh decreases in the low latitudes and global aggregate yields of rain-fed agriculture would increase by 5–20% in the first half of the 21st century. Warming exceeding this level would very likely see global declines in yields.[\[143\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-tab191-143)[\[144\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-ar4_summary_of_regional_impacts-144): 14–15  Since then, subsequent reports had been more negative on the global production potential.[\[6\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-AR6_WG2_Ch5-6)

The [US National Research Council](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/US_National_Research_Council "US National Research Council") assessed the literature on the effects of climate change on crop yields in 2011,[\[30\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-:6-30) and provided central estimates for key crops.[\[30\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-:6-30): 160  A meta-analysis in 2014 revealed consensus that yield is expected to decrease in the second half of the century, and with greater effect in tropical than temperate regions.[\[145\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-145)

A 2024 comprehensive review by Yuan et al. found that, under high-emission scenarios without adaptation, the combined effects of rising temperatures, altered precipitation patterns and increased evapotranspiration are projected to reduce yields of the four staple cereals (maize, rice, wheat and soybean) by approximately 5–10 % by mid-century.[\[146\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-146)

### Effects on yields for four major crops

\[ [edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture&action=edit&section=22 "Edit section: Effects on yields for four major crops")\]

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Projected_changes_in_yields_of_selected_crops_with_global_warming.png/250px-Projected_changes_in_yields_of_selected_crops_with_global_warming.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Projected_changes_in_yields_of_selected_crops_with_global_warming.png) US National Research Council projections of climate change effects on the most important crops.[\[30\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-:6-30): Figure 5.1

There is a large number of agricultural [crops](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Crop "Crop"), but not all of them are equally important. Most climate change assessments focus on "four major crops" – [maize](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Maize "Maize") (corn), [rice](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rice "Rice"), [wheat](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wheat "Wheat") and [soybeans](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soybeans "Soybeans")[\[147\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-147)– which are consumed directly and indirectly, as [animal feed](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Animal_feed "Animal feed") (the main purpose of soybeans). The three [cereals](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cereal "Cereal") are collectively responsible for half of the total human [calorie](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Calorie "Calorie") intake,[\[148\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-148) and together with soybeans, they account for two thirds.[\[12\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Zhao2017-12) Different methods have been used to project future yields of these crops, and by 2019, the consensus was that warming would lead to aggregate declines of the four. Maize and [soybean](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soybean "Soybean") would decrease with any warming, whereas rice and wheat production might peak at 3 °C (5.4 °F) of warming.[\[87\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Mbow_2019-87): 453

In 2021, a paper which used an ensemble of 21 [climate models](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_model "Climate model") estimated that under the most intense [climate change scenario](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change_scenario "Climate change scenario") used at the time, [RCP8.5](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Representative_Concentration_Pathway "Representative Concentration Pathway"), global yields of these four crops would decline by between 3–12% around 2050 and by 11–25% by the year 2100. The losses were concentrated in what are currently the major agricultural producers and exporters. For instance, even by 2050, some agricultural areas of [Australia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Australia "Australia"), [Brazil](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Brazil "Brazil"), [South Africa](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/South_Africa "South Africa"), [Southeast China](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Southeast_China "Southeast China"), [Southern Europe](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Southern_Europe "Southern Europe") and the [United States](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_States "United States") would suffer production losses of mostly maize and soybeans exceeding 25%.[\[149\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-149) A similar finding - that some major "breadbaskets" would begin to see unequivocal effects of climate change, both positive and negative, before the year 2040 - had been established in another study from the same year.[\[150\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-J%C3%A4germeyr2021-150) Since it represents the worst-case scenario of continually increasing emissions with no efforts to reduce them, RCP8.5 is often considered unrealistic,[\[151\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-HausfatherPeters2020-151) and a less intense RCP4.5 scenario (which still leads to nearly 3 °C (5.4 °F) by century's end, far in excess of the [Paris Agreement](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Paris_Agreement "Paris Agreement") goals) is now usually considered a better match for the current trajectory.[\[51\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Schuur2022-51)[\[52\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Phiddian2022-52)

In addition, reduced crop yields have the potential to increase food prices and negatively affect agricultural prosperity world-wide, with a 0.3% annual loss in prospective global GDP by 2100. This will negatively impact the means of subsistence for millions of people particularly in South Asian Nations. Over 70% of people in the Asian region depend on agriculture for their livelihood, it employs nearly 60 percent of the workforce and provides 22% of the gross domestic product (GDP) for the area.[\[152\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-ReferenceA-152)

#### Maize

\[ [edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture&action=edit&section=23 "Edit section: Maize")\]

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Maize_farming_in_drought_areas.jpg/250px-Maize_farming_in_drought_areas.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Maize_farming_in_drought_areas.jpg) [Maize](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Maize "Maize") farming in Uganda is made more difficult due to [heat waves](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Heat_wave "Heat wave") and [droughts](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Drought "Drought") worsened by [climate change in Uganda](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change_in_Uganda "Climate change in Uganda").

Out of the four crops, maize is considered the most vulnerable to warming,[\[153\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-153) with one meta-analysis concluding that every 1 °C (1.8 °F) of global warming reduces maize yields by 7.4%.[\[12\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Zhao2017-12)

It is also a [C4 carbon fixation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/C4_carbon_fixation "C4 carbon fixation") plant, meaning that it experiences little benefit from the increased CO2 levels.[\[10\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Ainsworth2021-10) When the results from modelling experiments comparing the combined output of latest [earth system models](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth_system_model "Earth system model") and dedicated agricultural crop models were published in 2021, the most notable new finding was the substantial reduction in projected global yields of maize. While the previous generation suggested that under the low-warming scenario, maize productivity would increase by around 5% by the end of the century, the latest had shown a reduction of 6% under the equivalent scenario, [SSP1-2.6](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shared_Socioeconomic_Pathways "Shared Socioeconomic Pathways"). Under the high-emission SSP5-8.5, there was a global decline of 24% by 2100, as opposed to the earlier suggestion of a 1% increase.[\[150\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-J%C3%A4germeyr2021-150)

#### Rice

\[ [edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture&action=edit&section=24 "Edit section: Rice")\]

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Parched_rice_field_Can_Tho.JPG/250px-Parched_rice_field_Can_Tho.JPG)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Parched_rice_field_Can_Tho.JPG) A rice field suffering from the effects of drought in [Binh Thuy District](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/B%C3%ACnh_Th%E1%BB%A7y_district "Bình Thủy district"), Can Tho, Vietnam.

Studies indicate that on their own, temperature changes reduce global rice yields by 3.2% for every 1 °C (1.8 °F) of global warming.[\[12\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Zhao2017-12) Projections become more complicated once the changes in precipitation, CO2 fertilization effect and other factors need to be taken into account: for instance, climate effects on rice growth in [East Asia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/East_Asia "East Asia") had been a net positive so far,[\[6\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-AR6_WG2_Ch5-6): 728  although 2023 research suggested that by the end of the century, [China](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/China "China") could lose up to 8% of its rice yield due to increases in extreme rainfall events alone.[\[29\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Fu2023-29) As of 2021, global projections of rice yields from the most advanced climate and agricultural models were less consistent than they were for wheat and maize, and less able to identify a clear statistically significant trend.[\[150\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-J%C3%A4germeyr2021-150)

This section is an excerpt from [Rice § Effects of climate change on rice production](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rice#Effects_of_climate_change_on_rice_production "Rice").\[ [edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Rice&action=edit)\]

Predictions of climate change's effects on rice cultivation vary. Global rice yield has been projected to decrease by around 3.2% with each 1°C increase in global average temperature[\[154\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-154) while another study predicts global rice cultivation will increase initially, plateauing at about 3°C warming (2091–2100 relative to 1850–1900).[\[155\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-155)

The impacts of climate change on rice cultivation vary across geographic location and socioeconomic context. For example, rising temperatures and decreasing solar radiation during the later years of the 20th century decreased rice yield by between 10% and 20% across 200 farms in seven Asian countries. This may have been caused by increased night-time respiration.[\[156\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Rice_Welch_Vincent_Auffhammer_Moya_2010-156)[\[157\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Rice_Black_2010-157) IRRI has predicted that Asian rice yields will fall by some 20% per 1°C rise in global mean temperature. Further, rice is unable to yield grain if the flowers experience a temperature of 35°C or more for over one hour, so the crop would be lost under these conditions.[\[158\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Rice_Singh_2016-158)[\[159\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Rice_Rao_Patil_2017-159)

In the [Po Valley](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Po_Valley "Po Valley") in Italy, the [arborio](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arborio_rice "Arborio rice") and [carnaroli](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carnaroli "Carnaroli") risotto rice varieties have suffered poor harvests through drought in the 21st century. The [Ente Nazionale Risi](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Ente_Nazionale_Risi&action=edit&redlink=1 "Ente Nazionale Risi (page does not exist)") \[ [it](https://it.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ente_Nazionale_Risi "it:Ente Nazionale Risi")\] is developing drought-resistant varieties; its _nuovo prometeo_ variety has deep roots that enable it to tolerate drought, but is not suitable for risotto.[\[160\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Rice_Spaggiari_2024-160)

#### Wheat

\[ [edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture&action=edit&section=25 "Edit section: Wheat")\]

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Results_of_heavy_rainfalls_II.jpg/250px-Results_of_heavy_rainfalls_II.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Results_of_heavy_rainfalls_II.jpg) Wheat field in [Hungary](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hungary "Hungary") which had been affected by heavy rainfall in 2016.

Further information: [Wheat](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wheat "Wheat") and [Winter Wheat](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Winter_Wheat "Winter Wheat")

Climate change effects on [rainfed](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rainfed_agriculture "Rainfed agriculture") [wheat](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wheat "Wheat") will vary depending on the region and local climatic conditions. Studies in [Iran](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iran "Iran") surrounding changes in temperature and rainfall are representative for several different parts of the world since there exists a wide range of climatic conditions. They range from temperate to hot-arid to cold semi-arid. Scenarios based on increasing temperature by up to 2.5 °C (4.5 °F) and rainfall decreases by up to 25% show wheat grain yield losses can be significant. The losses can be as much as 45% in [temperate areas](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Temperate_climate "Temperate climate") and over 50% in hot-arid areas. But in cold semi-arid areas yields can be increased somewhat (about 15%). Adaptation strategies with the most promise center around dates for seed planting. Late planting in November to January can have significant positive effects on yields due to the seasonality of rainfall.[\[161\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-161) However, those experiments did not consider the effects of CO2 increases.

Globally, temperature changes alone are expected to reduce annual wheat yield by 6% for every 1 °C (1.8 °F) of global warming.[\[12\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Zhao2017-12) However, other factors such as precipitation and the CO2 fertilization effect benefit wheat yields far more. In November 2021, the results from modelling experiments comparing the combined output of latest [earth system models](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth_system_model "Earth system model") and dedicated agricultural crop models were published. While it projected a consistent decrease in future global yields of maize, particularly under greater warming, it found the opposite for wheat yields. When the previous generation of models suggested a 9% increase in global wheat yields by 2100 under the high-emission scenario, the updated results indicate that under its highest-warming SSP5-8.5 scenario, they would increase by 18%.[\[150\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-J%C3%A4germeyr2021-150) As per projections, heat-induced stress could potentially affect approximately half of the Indo-Gangetic Plains (IGP), making it unsuitable for the cultivation of wheat by 2050.[\[152\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-ReferenceA-152)

#### Soybeans

\[ [edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture&action=edit&section=26 "Edit section: Soybeans")\]

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Piezodorus_guildinii_02.jpg/250px-Piezodorus_guildinii_02.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Piezodorus_guildinii_02.jpg) [Piezodorus guildinii](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Piezodorus_guildinii "Piezodorus guildinii"), commonly known as red-banded stink bug, is a notable soybean pest which is expected to spread further and cause more damage in response to climate change.

Studies have shown that when CO2 levels rise, [soybean](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soybean "Soybean") leaves are less nutritious; therefore plant-eating beetles have to eat more to get their required [nutrients](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nutrients "Nutrients").[\[34\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Ferber_&_Epstein-34) In addition, soybeans are less capable of defending themselves against the [predatory insects](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Predatory_insect "Predatory insect") under high CO2. The CO2 diminishes the plant's [jasmonic acid](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jasmonic_acid "Jasmonic acid") production, an insect-killing poison that is excreted when the plant senses it is being attacked. Without this protection, beetles are able to eat the soybean leaves freely, resulting in a lower crop yield.[\[34\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Ferber_&_Epstein-34) This is not a problem unique to soybeans, and many plant species' defence mechanisms are impaired in a high CO2 environment.[\[128\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Chakra-128)

Studies indicate that on their own, temperature changes reduce global soybean yields by 3.1% for every 1 °C (1.8 °F) of global warming.[\[12\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Zhao2017-12) These projections become more complicated once the changes in precipitation, CO2 fertilization effect and other factors need to be taken into account: as of 2021, global projections of soybean yields from the most advanced climate and agricultural models were less able to establish a strong trend when compared to the projections for maize and wheat.[\[150\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-J%C3%A4germeyr2021-150)

### Other crops

\[ [edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture&action=edit&section=27 "Edit section: Other crops")\]

Climate change induced by increasing [greenhouse gases](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Greenhouse_gas "Greenhouse gas") is likely to differ across crops and countries.[\[162\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-162)

#### Millet and sorghum

\[ [edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture&action=edit&section=28 "Edit section: Millet and sorghum")\]

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/Sorghum_field_at_the_shores_of_Lake_Hayq_Ethiopia.jpg/250px-Sorghum_field_at_the_shores_of_Lake_Hayq_Ethiopia.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Sorghum_field_at_the_shores_of_Lake_Hayq_Ethiopia.jpg) A sorghum field at the shores of [Lake Hayq](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lake_Hayq "Lake Hayq") in [Ethiopia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ethiopia "Ethiopia").

[Millet](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Millet "Millet") and [sorghum](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sorghum "Sorghum") are not as widely consumed as the four major crops, but they are crucial [staples](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Staple_crop "Staple crop") in many [African](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Africa "Africa") countries.
