A paper published in the year 2022 found that under the highest-warming [SSP5-8.5](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shared_Socioeconomic_Pathways "Shared Socioeconomic Pathways") scenario, changes in temperature and [soil moisture](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soil_moisture "Soil moisture") would reduce the aggregate yields of millet, sorghum, maize and soybeans by between 9% and 32%, depending on the model. Notably, this was a less pessimistic result than in the earlier models, which the authors attributed to simulating soil moisture directly, rather than attempting to indirectly account for it by tracking [precipitation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Precipitation "Precipitation") changes caused by [effects of climate change on the water cycle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_the_water_cycle "Effects of climate change on the water cycle").[\[163\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-163)

#### Lentils (besides soybeans)

\[ [edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture&action=edit&section=29 "Edit section: Lentils (besides soybeans)")\]

Climate change induced drought stress in Africa will likely lead to a reduction in the nutritional quality of the common bean.[\[164\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-164) This would primarily impact populations in poorer countries less able to compensate by eating more food, more varied diets, or possibly taking supplements.

#### Potatoes

\[ [edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture&action=edit&section=30 "Edit section: Potatoes")\]

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Gervais_2021_potato_drought_response.jpg/250px-Gervais_2021_potato_drought_response.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Gervais_2021_potato_drought_response.jpg) Response of six prominent potato varieties to drought conditions.[\[165\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Gervais2021-165)

This section is an excerpt from [Potato § Effects of climate change on production](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Potato#Effects_of_climate_change_on_production "Potato").\[ [edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Potato&action=edit)\]

Potato plants and crop yields are predicted to benefit from the [CO2 fertilization effect](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/CO2_fertilization_effect "CO2 fertilization effect"),[\[166\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Potato_UK-166) which would increase [photosynthetic rates](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Photosynthesis "Photosynthesis") and therefore growth, reduce water consumption through lower [transpiration](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Transpiration "Transpiration") from [stomata](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stomata "Stomata") and increase starch content in the edible tubers.[\[167\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Potato_supply-167) However, potatoes are more sensitive to soil water deficits than some other staple crops like wheat.[\[168\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-168) In the UK, the amount of [arable land](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arable_land "Arable land") suitable for [rainfed](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rainfed "Rainfed") potato production is predicted to decrease by at least 75%.[\[169\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-169) These changes are likely to lead to increased demand for [irrigation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Irrigation "Irrigation") water, particularly during the potato growing season.[\[167\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Potato_supply-167)

Potatoes grow best under temperate conditions.[\[170\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Potato_global-170) Temperatures above 30 °C (86 °F) have negative effects on potato crops, from physiological damage such as brown spots on tubers, to slower growth, premature sprouting, and lower starch content.[\[171\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Potato_Levy-171) These effects reduce crop yield, affecting both the number and the weight of tubers. As a result, areas where current temperatures are near the limits of potatoes' temperature range (e.g. much of [sub-Saharan Africa](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sub-Saharan_Africa "Sub-Saharan Africa"))[\[167\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Potato_supply-167) will likely suffer large reductions in potato crop yields in the future.[\[170\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Potato_global-170) On the other hand, low temperatures reduce potato growth and present risk of frost damage.[\[167\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Potato_supply-167)

As well as affecting potatoes directly, climate change will also affect the distributions and populations of many potato diseases and pests. For instance, [late blight](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Late_blight "Late blight") is predicted to become a greater threat in some areas (e.g. in [Finland](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Finland "Finland")[\[123\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-supply-123)) and become a lesser threat in others (e.g. in the [United Kingdom](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_Kingdom "United Kingdom")[\[172\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-UK-172) Altogether, one 2003 estimate suggests that future (2040–2069) worldwide potato yield would be 18–32% lower than it was at the time, driven by declines in hotter areas like Sub-Saharan Africa,[\[123\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-supply-123) unless farmers and potato cultivars can adapt to the new environment.[\[116\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Luck-et-al-2011-116)

#### Grapevines (wine production)

\[ [edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture&action=edit&section=31 "Edit section: Grapevines (wine production)")\]

This section is an excerpt from [Vitis vinifera § Climate change](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vitis_vinifera#Climate_change "Vitis vinifera").\[ [edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Vitis_vinifera&action=edit)\]

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Damaged_and_sunburnt_Chardonnay_clusters.JPG/250px-Damaged_and_sunburnt_Chardonnay_clusters.JPG)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Damaged_and_sunburnt_Chardonnay_clusters.JPG) [Chardonnay](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chardonnay "Chardonnay") grapes which had been damaged by heat from sunburn

Grapevines are very responsive to their surrounding environment with a seasonal variation in yield of 32.5%.[\[173\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Vitis_vinifera_Chloupek2004-173) Climate is one of the key controlling factors in grape and wine production,[\[174\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Vitis_vinifera_Fraga2014-174) affecting the suitability of certain grape varieties to a particular region as well as the type and quality of the wine produced.[\[175\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Vitis_vinifera_Gladstones1992-175)[\[176\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Vitis_vinifera_Fraga2015-176) Wine composition is largely dependent on the [mesoclimate](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mesoclimate "Mesoclimate") and the [microclimate](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Microclimate "Microclimate") and this means that for high quality wines to be produced, a climate-soil-variety equilibrium has to be maintained. The interaction between climate-soil-variety will in some cases come under threat from the [effects of climate change](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change "Effects of climate change"). Identification of genes underlying [phenological](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Phenology "Phenology") variation in grape may help to maintain consistent yield of particular varieties in future climatic conditions.[\[177\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Vitis_vinifera_Grzeskowiak2013-177)

The gradually increasing temperatures will lead to a shift in suitable growing regions.[\[178\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Vitis_vinifera_Fraga2016-178) It is estimated that the northern boundary of European viticulture will shift north 10 to 30 kilometres (6.2 to 18.6 mi) per decade up to 2020 with a doubling of this rate predicted between 2020 and 2050.[\[179\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Vitis_vinifera_Kenny1993-179) This has positive and negative effects, as it opens doors to new cultivars being grown in certain regions but a loss of suitability of other cultivars and may also risk production quality and quantity in general.[\[180\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Vitis_vinifera_Kovacs2014-180)[\[178\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Vitis_vinifera_Fraga2016-178)

## Effects on livestock rearing

\[ [edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture&action=edit&section=32 "Edit section: Effects on livestock rearing")\]

This section is an excerpt from [Effects of climate change on livestock](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_livestock "Effects of climate change on livestock").\[ [edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Effects_of_climate_change_on_livestock&action=edit)\]

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Godber_2014_livestock_impacts_map.jpg/500px-Godber_2014_livestock_impacts_map.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Godber_2014_livestock_impacts_map.jpg) A map of countries considered most and least vulnerable to adverse impacts of climate change on their grazing livestock[\[181\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Effects_of_climate_change_on_livestock_Godber2014-181)[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Lacetera_2018_climate_livestock_diagram.jpeg/500px-Lacetera_2018_climate_livestock_diagram.jpeg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Lacetera_2018_climate_livestock_diagram.jpeg) Multi-faceted [impacts of climate change](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change "Effects of climate change") on livestock[\[46\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Effects_of_climate_change_on_livestock_Lacetera2018-46)

There are numerous interlinked [effects of climate change on livestock](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_livestock "Effects of climate change on livestock") rearing. This activity is both heavily affected by and a substantial driver of anthropogenic [climate change](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change "Climate change") due to its [greenhouse gas emissions](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Greenhouse_gas_emissions_from_agriculture#Livestock "Greenhouse gas emissions from agriculture"). As of 2011, some 400 million people relied on livestock in some way to secure their livelihood.[\[44\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Effects_of_climate_change_on_livestock_AR6_WGII_Chapter5-44): 746  The commercial value of this sector is estimated as close to $1 [trillion](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Trillion "Trillion").[\[182\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-182) As an outright end to human consumption of meat and animal products is not currently considered a realistic goal,[\[183\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-183) any comprehensive [adaptation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change_adaptation "Climate change adaptation") to [effects of climate change](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change "Effects of climate change") must also consider livestock.

The observed adverse impacts on [livestock production](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Livestock_production "Livestock production") include increased [heat stress](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Heat_stress "Heat stress") in all but the coldest nations.[\[45\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Effects_of_climate_change_on_livestock_Zhang2023-45)[\[184\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Effects_of_climate_change_on_livestock_Liu2024-184) This causes both mass animal mortality during [heatwaves](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Heatwave "Heatwave"), and the sublethal impacts, such as lower quantity of quality of products like [milk](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milk "Milk"), greater vulnerability to conditions like [lameness](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lameness "Lameness") or even impaired [reproduction](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Reproduction "Reproduction").[\[44\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Effects_of_climate_change_on_livestock_AR6_WGII_Chapter5-44) Another impact concerns reduced quantity or quality of [animal feed](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Animal_feed "Animal feed"), whether due to drought or as a secondary impact of [CO2 fertilization effect](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/CO2_fertilization_effect "CO2 fertilization effect"). Difficulties with growing feed could reduce worldwide livestock headcounts by 7–10% by midcentury.[\[44\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Effects_of_climate_change_on_livestock_AR6_WGII_Chapter5-44): 748  Animal [parasites](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Parasite "Parasite") and [vector-borne diseases](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vector-borne_disease "Vector-borne disease") are also spreading further than they had before, and the data indicating this is frequently of superior quality to one used to estimate impacts on the spread of human pathogens.[\[44\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Effects_of_climate_change_on_livestock_AR6_WGII_Chapter5-44)

While some areas which currently support livestock animals are expected to avoid "extreme heat stress" even with high warming at the end of the century, others may stop being suitable as early as midcentury.[\[44\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Effects_of_climate_change_on_livestock_AR6_WGII_Chapter5-44): 750  In general, [sub-Saharan Africa](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sub-Saharan_Africa "Sub-Saharan Africa") is considered to be the most vulnerable region to [food security](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Food_security "Food security") shocks caused by the impacts of climate change on their livestock, as over 180 million people across those nations are expected to see significant declines in suitability of their [rangelands](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rangelands "Rangelands") around midcentury.[\[44\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Effects_of_climate_change_on_livestock_AR6_WGII_Chapter5-44): 748  On the other hand, Japan, the United States and nations in Europe are considered the least vulnerable. This is as much a product of pre-existing differences in [human development index](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_development_index "Human development index") and other measures of [national resilience](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Community_resilience "Community resilience") and widely varying importance of [pastoralism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pastoralism "Pastoralism") to the national diet as it is an outcome of direct impacts of climate on each country.[\[181\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Effects_of_climate_change_on_livestock_Godber2014-181)

## Global food security and undernutrition

\[ [edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture&action=edit&section=33 "Edit section: Global food security and undernutrition")\]

See also: [Effects of climate change on fisheries](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_fisheries "Effects of climate change on fisheries")

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Hasegawa_2016_hunger_DALYs.png/500px-Hasegawa_2016_hunger_DALYs.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Hasegawa_2016_hunger_DALYs.png) Projected changes in average food availability (represented as calorie consumption [per capita](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Per_capita "Per capita")), population at risk of hunger and [disability-adjusted life years](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Disability-adjusted_life_years "Disability-adjusted life years") under two [Shared Socioeconomic Pathways](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shared_Socioeconomic_Pathways "Shared Socioeconomic Pathways"): the baseline, SSP2, and SSP3, scenario of high global rivalry and conflict. The red and the orange lines show projections for SSP3 assuming high and low intensity of future emissions and the associated climate change.[\[185\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Hasegawa2016-185)

Scientific understanding of how climate change would affect global [food security](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Food_security "Food security") has evolved over time. The latest [IPCC Sixth Assessment Report](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/IPCC_Sixth_Assessment_Report "IPCC Sixth Assessment Report") in 2022 suggested that by 2050, the number of people at risk of hunger will increase under all scenarios by between 8 and 80 million people, with nearly all of them in [Sub-Saharan Africa](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sub-Saharan_Africa "Sub-Saharan Africa"), [South Asia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/South_Asia "South Asia") and [Central America](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Central_America "Central America"). However, this comparison was done relative to a world where no climate change had occurred, and so it does not rule out the possibility of an overall reduction in hunger risk when compared to present-day conditions.[\[6\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-AR6_WG2_Ch5-6): 717

The earlier [Special Report on Climate Change and Land](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Special_Report_on_Climate_Change_and_Land "Special Report on Climate Change and Land") suggested that under a relatively high emission scenario (RCP6.0), cereals may become 1–29% more expensive in 2050 depending on the socioeconomic pathway.[\[87\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Mbow_2019-87): 439  Compared to a scenario where climate change is absent, this would put between 1–181 million people with low income at risk of hunger.[\[87\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Mbow_2019-87)

It is difficult to project the effect of climate change on utilization (protecting food against spoilage, being healthy enough to absorb nutrients, etc.). In 2016, a modelling study suggested that by mid-century, the most intense climate change scenario would reduce per capita global food availability by 3.2%, with a 0.7% decrease in [red meat](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Red_meat "Red meat") consumption and a 4% decrease in [fruit](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fruit "Fruit") and [vegetable](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vegetable "Vegetable") consumption. According to its numbers, 529,000 people would die between 2010 and 2050 as the result, primarily in [South Asia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/South_Asia "South Asia") and [East Asia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/East_Asia "East Asia"): two-thirds of those deaths would be caused by the lack of [micronutrients](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Micronutrient "Micronutrient") from reduced [fruit](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fruit "Fruit") and [vegetable](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vegetable "Vegetable") supply, rather than outright starvation. Acting to slow climate change would reduce these projections by up to 71%.[\[186\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Springmann2016-186) [Food prices](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Food_prices "Food prices") are also expected to become more volatile.[\[187\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-187)

As of 2017, around 821 million people had suffered from hunger. This was equivalent to about 11% of the world population: regionally, this included 23.2% of [sub-Saharan Africa](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sub-Saharan_Africa "Sub-Saharan Africa"), 16.5% of the [Caribbean](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Caribbean "Caribbean") and 14.8% of [South Asia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/South_Asia "South Asia").[\[15\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Janssens2020-15) In 2021, 720 million to 811 million people were considered undernourished in 2021 (of whom 200,000, 32.3 million and 112.3 million people were at a "catastrophic", "emergency" and "crisis" levels of food insecurity, respectively).[\[14\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-FAO2021-14)

In 2020, research suggested that the baseline projected level of socioeconomic development ( [Shared Socioeconomic Pathway 2](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shared_Socioeconomic_Pathways "Shared Socioeconomic Pathways")) would reduce this number to 122 million globally by 2050, even as the population grows to reach 9.2 billion. The effect of climate change would at most increase this 2050 figure by around 80 million, and the negative effect could be reduced to 20 million through enabling easier food trade with measures such as eliminating [tariffs](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tariff "Tariff").[\[15\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Janssens2020-15)

In 2021, a [meta-analysis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Meta-analysis "Meta-analysis") of 57 studies on food security was more pessimistic, suggesting that the year 2050 population at risk of hunger would be around 500 million under SSP2. Some variations of [Shared Socioeconomic Pathways](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shared_Socioeconomic_Pathways "Shared Socioeconomic Pathways") with high climate change and a lack of equitable [global development](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Global_development "Global development") instead resulted in an outright increase of global hunger by up to 30% from its 2010 levels.[\[13\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-vanDijk2021-13)

For the earlier [IPCC Fourth Assessment Report](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/IPCC_Fourth_Assessment_Report "IPCC Fourth Assessment Report") in 2007, the analysis of four main [SRES](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Special_Report_on_Emissions_Scenarios "Special Report on Emissions Scenarios") pathways had shown with _medium confidence_ (about 50% certainty)[\[188\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-parry_uncertainty_assessment-188)) that trends of [social](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Social_change "Social change") and [economic development](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Economic_development "Economic development") in three of them (A1, B1, B2) would see the number of undernourished people decline to 100–130 million people by the year 2080, while trends in A2 projected 770 million undernourished – similar to the contemporary (early 21st century) figures of ~700 million people. Once the effect of climate change implied by those scenarios was taken into account, A1, B1 and B2 scenarios would see 100–380 million undernourished by 2080 (still a major decline in [hunger](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hunger "Hunger") from 2006 levels), and A2 would see 740–1,300 million, although there was only _low_ (20% certainty) to _medium_ certainty in these figures.[\[189\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-easterling_food_security-189) [Sub-Saharan Africa](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sub-Saharan_Africa "Sub-Saharan Africa") would likely overtake [Asia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Asia "Asia") as the world's most food-insecure region, primarily due to differing socioeconomic trends.[\[190\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-easterling_sub_saharan_africa-190)

### Effects of extreme weather and synchronized crop failures

\[ [edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture&action=edit&section=34 "Edit section: Effects of extreme weather and synchronized crop failures")\]

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Kummu_2021_zones.jpg/500px-Kummu_2021_zones.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Kummu_2021_zones.jpg) Areas of the globe where agriculture would become more difficult perhaps to the point of leaving the conditions historically suitable for it, under low-emission and high-emission scenarios, by 2100.[\[18\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Kummu2021-18)

Further information: [Multiple breadbasket failure](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Multiple_breadbasket_failure "Multiple breadbasket failure")

Some scientists consider the aforementioned projections of crop yields and food security of limited use, because in their view, they primarily model climate change as a change in mean climate state, and are not as well-equipped to consider climatic extremes. For instance, a paper published in 2021 had also attempted to calculate the number of people facing hunger in 2050 – but now on the assumption that a climate event with a 1% (i.e. once in 100 years) likelihood of occurring in the _new_ climate (meaning it would have been effectively impossible in the present climate) were to impact that year. It estimated that such an event would increase the baseline number by 11–33% even in the low-emission scenario, and by 20–36% in the high-emission one. If such an event were to affect more vulnerable regions like [South Asia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/South_Asia "South Asia"), then they would have required triple their 2021 level of known food reserves to absorb the blow.[\[16\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Hasegawa2021-16)

Notably, other papers show that simulating recent historical extreme events in climate models, such as the [2003 European heatwave](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/2003_European_heatwave "2003 European heatwave"), typically results in lower effects than what had been observed in the real world, indicating that the effects of future extreme events are also likely to be underestimated.[\[17\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Schewe2019-17)[\[191\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Heinicke2022-191)

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Projections_for_crop_suitability_to_2100_and_2500_under_the_moderate%E2%80%93high_RCP6.0_emission_scenario.webp/500px-Projections_for_crop_suitability_to_2100_and_2500_under_the_moderate%E2%80%93high_RCP6.0_emission_scenario.webp.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Projections_for_crop_suitability_to_2100_and_2500_under_the_moderate%E2%80%93high_RCP6.0_emission_scenario.webp) Projections of crop yields for seven key crops in year 2100 and year 2500 under the second-highest warming scenario, [RCP6.0](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Representative_Concentration_Pathway "Representative Concentration Pathway"). (Research from 2021.)[\[192\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Lyon2021-192)

The difference between climatic mean and extremes may be particularly important for determining areas where agriculture may stop being viable. In 2021, a research team aimed to extend [climate model](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_model "Climate model") projections of mean changes in temperature and [the water cycle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_the_water_cycle "Effects of climate change on the water cycle") to the year 2500. They suggested that under the second-strongest warming scenario RCP6.0, land area capable of supporting four major temperate crops (maize, potato, soybean and wheat) would become about 11% smaller by 2100 and 18.3% smaller by 2500, while for major tropical crops ( [cassava](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cassava "Cassava"), rice, [sweet potato](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sweet_potato "Sweet potato"), [sorghum](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sorghum "Sorghum"), [taro](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Taro "Taro"), and [yam](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Yam_(vegetable) "Yam (vegetable)")), it would decline by only 2.3% around 2100, yet by around 15% by 2500. Under the low-emission scenario RCP2.6, changes are much smaller, with around 3% decline in suitable land area for temperate crops by 2500 and an equivalent gain for tropical crops by then.[\[192\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Lyon2021-192)

Yet, another paper from 2021 suggested that by 2100, under the high-emission SSP5-8.5, 31% and 34% of the current crop and livestock production would leave what the authors have defined as a "safe climatic space": that is, those areas (most of [South Asia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/South_Asia "South Asia") and the [Middle East](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Middle_East "Middle East"), as well as parts of [sub-Saharan Africa](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sub-Saharan_Africa "Sub-Saharan Africa") and [Central America](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Central_America "Central America")) would experience very rapid shift in [Holdridge life zones](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Holdridge_life_zones "Holdridge life zones") (HLZ) and associated weather, while also being low in social resilience. Notably, a similar fraction of global crop and livestock production would also experience a large change in HLZ, but in more developed areas which would have better chances of adapting. In contrast, under the low-emissions SSP1-2.6, 5% and 8% of crop and livestock production would leave what is defined as the safe climatic space.[\[18\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Kummu2021-18)

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Food_supply_teleconnections_2016.jpg/250px-Food_supply_teleconnections_2016.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Food_supply_teleconnections_2016.jpg) Certain countries around the world are particularly dependent on imports from specific exporters, leaving them the most vulnerable to crop failures in those countries.[\[193\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-d'Amour2016-193)

Also in 2021, it was suggested that the high-emission scenario would result in a 4.5-fold increase in the probability of breadbasket failures (defined as a yield loss of 10% or more) by 2030, which could then increase 25 times by 2050.[\[194\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Caparas2021-194) This corresponds to reaching 1.5 °C (2.7 °F) and 2 °C (3.6 °F) thresholds under that scenario: earlier research suggested that for maize, this would increase risks for [multiple simultaneous breadbasket failures](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Multiple_breadbasket_failure "Multiple breadbasket failure") (yield loss of 10% or more) from 6% under the late-20th century climate to 40% and 54%, respectively.[\[8\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Gaupp2019-8)

Some countries are particularly dependent on imports from certain exporters, meaning that a crop failure in those countries would hit them disproportionately. I.e. a ban on export of staple crops from [Russia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Russia "Russia"), [Thailand](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Thailand "Thailand") and the [United States](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_States "United States") alone would place around 200 million people (90% from Sub-Saharan Africa) at risk of starvation.[\[193\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-d'Amour2016-193)

Additionally, there is the issue of representing synchronization - where extreme climate events happen to strike multiple important producer regions around the same time. It was estimated that if hypothetically, every region with a synchronized growing season were to experience crop failure at the same time, it would cause losses of four major crops between 17% and 34%.[\[195\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-195) More realistically, analysis of historic data suggested that there have already been synchronized climate events associated with up to 20% yield losses.[\[196\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-196)

According to a 2016 estimate, if global maize, rice and wheat exports declined by 10%, 55 million people in 58 poor countries lose at least 5% of their food supply.[\[193\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-d'Amour2016-193) Further, two specific [Rossby wave](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rossby_wave "Rossby wave") pattern are known to induce simultaneous heat extremes in either [Eastern Asia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Eastern_Asia "Eastern Asia"), [Eastern Europe](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Eastern_Europe "Eastern Europe") and Central North America, or in [Western Asia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Western_Asia "Western Asia"), [Western Europe](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Western_Europe "Western Europe") and Western Central North America, respectively. These heat extremes have already been shown to cause 3–4% declines in crop yield across the affected regions:[\[197\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-197) yet, concerningly, climate models overestimate the effects of such historic events in North America and underestimate them elsewhere, simulating effectively no net yield loss.[\[9\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Kornhuber2023-9)

### Labour and economic effects

\[ [edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture&action=edit&section=35 "Edit section: Labour and economic effects")\]

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/Kang_2018_NCP_irrigation_RCPs.png/250px-Kang_2018_NCP_irrigation_RCPs.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Kang_2018_NCP_irrigation_RCPs.png) Climate change is expected to exacerbate [heat stress](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Heat_stress "Heat stress") over at the [North China Plain](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/North_China_Plain "North China Plain"), which is particularly vulnerable as widespread [irrigation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Irrigation "Irrigation") results in very moist air. There is a risk that agricultural labourers will be physically unable to work outdoors on hot summer days, particularly under the scenario of greatest emissions and warming.[\[198\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Kang2018-198)[\[199\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-199)

As [extreme weather events](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Extreme_weather "Extreme weather") become more common and more intense, floods and droughts can destroy crops and eliminate food supply, while disrupting agricultural activities and rendering workers jobless.[\[40\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Canada-40)[\[200\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Kristjanson2-200) With more costs to the farmer, some will no longer find it financially feasible to farm: i.e. some farmers may choose to permanently leave drought-affected areas.[\[201\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-201) [Agriculture](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Agriculture "Agriculture") employs the majority of the population in most low-income countries and increased costs can result in worker layoffs or pay cuts.[\[58\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Hertel2-58) Other farmers will respond by raising their [food prices](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Food_prices "Food prices"); a cost that is directly passed on to the consumer and affects the affordability of food. Some farms do not sell their produce but instead feed a family or community; without that food, people will not have enough to eat. This results in decreased production, increased food prices, and potential starvation in parts of the world.[\[202\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Beddington-202) The agriculture industry in [India](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/India "India") makes up 52% of their employment and the [Canadian Prairies](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Canadian_Prairies "Canadian Prairies") supply 51% of Canadian agriculture; any changes in the production of food crops from these areas could have profound effects on the [economy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Economy "Economy").[\[39\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Kul2-39)

Notably, one estimate suggests that a warming of 3 °C (5.4 °F) relative to late 20th century (i.e. closer to 4 °C (7.2 °F) when compared to preindustrial temperatures – a level associated with the SSP5-8.5 scenario) would cause [labour capacity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_human_health#Reduced_labour_capacity "Effects of climate change on human health") in [Sub-Saharan Africa](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sub-Saharan_Africa "Sub-Saharan Africa") and [Southeast Asia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Southeast_Asia "Southeast Asia") to decline by 30 to 50%, as the number of days when outdoor workers experience [heat stress](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Heat_stress "Heat stress") increases: up to 250 days the worst-affected parts of these two continents and of [Central](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Central_America "Central America") and [South America](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/South_America "South America"). This could then increase crop prices by around 5%.[\[6\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-AR6_WG2_Ch5-6): 717 : 725

Similarly, [North China Plain](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/North_China_Plain "North China Plain") is also expected to be highly affected, in part due to the region's extensive [irrigation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Irrigation "Irrigation") networks resulting in unusually moist air. In scenarios without aggressive [action to stop climate change](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change_mitigation "Climate change mitigation"), some heatwaves could become extreme enough to cause mass mortality in outdoor labourers, although they will remain relatively uncommon (up to around once per decade starting from 2100 under the most extreme scenario).[\[198\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Kang2018-198)

Further, the role of climate change in undernutrition and micronutrient deficiencies can be calculated as the loss of "years of full health".[\[6\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-AR6_WG2_Ch5-6): 717 One estimate presented in 2016 suggests that under the scenario of strong warming and low adaptation due to high global conflict and rivalry, such losses may take up 0.4% of the global [GDP](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/GDP "GDP") and 4% of the GDP in India and the [South Asian](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/South_Asia "South Asia") region by the year 2100.[\[185\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Hasegawa2016-185)

Global warming intensifies agriculture’s existing harms such as soil loss, pollution, and greenhouse gas emissions by reducing crop yields and forcing greater land use and input dependence. It emphasizes that climate change and agriculture create a vicious feedback loop, making sustainable food production increasingly difficult unless farming systems become more resilient and climate-friendly.[\[203\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-203)

### Long-term predictions (beyond 2050)

\[ [edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture&action=edit&section=36 "Edit section: Long-term predictions (beyond 2050)")\]

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Mondal_2024_South_Asia_cropland_extent.jpg/250px-Mondal_2024_South_Asia_cropland_extent.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Mondal_2024_South_Asia_cropland_extent.jpg) In [South Asia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/South_Asia "South Asia"), the extent of cropland is expected to increase under most climate and socioeconomic scenarios, with some of the largest increases seen under high-warming scenarios. Low-warming scenarios are expected to see a decrease in area due to lower demand.[\[204\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Mondal2024-204) Similar trends are expected globally.[\[86\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Riahi2017-86)

There are fewer projections looking beyond 2050. In general, even as climate change would cause increasingly severe effects on food production, most scientists do not anticipate it to result in mass human mortality within this century.[\[20\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Bradshaw2021-20)[\[19\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-WG3_Chapter3-19) This is in part because the studies also anticipate at least some continuation of the ongoing agricultural improvements, yet also because of [agricultural expansion](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Agricultural_expansion "Agricultural expansion"). For instance, a 2013 paper estimated that if the high warming of [RCP 8.5](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Representative_Concentration_Pathway "Representative Concentration Pathway") scenario was not alleviated by CO2 fertilization effect, it would reduce aggregate yields by 17% by the year 2050: yet, it anticipated that this would be mostly offset through an 11% increase in [cropland](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cropland "Cropland") area.[\[142\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Nelson2013-142)

Similarly, one of the assumptions of [Shared Socioeconomic Pathways](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shared_Socioeconomic_Pathways "Shared Socioeconomic Pathways") is a significant increase in land allocated to agriculture (and a corresponding decrease in [forest](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Forest "Forest") and "other natural land" area) in every pathway besides the SSP1 (officially subtitled " [Sustainability](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sustainability "Sustainability")" or "Taking the Green Road"), where the inverse occurs – and which has both the lowest level of future warming and the lowest projected [population growth](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Population_growth "Population growth").[\[86\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Riahi2017-86)

## Regional effects

\[ [edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture&action=edit&section=37 "Edit section: Regional effects")\]

See also: [Effects of climate change](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change "Effects of climate change")

### Africa

\[ [edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture&action=edit&section=38 "Edit section: Africa")\]

This section is an excerpt from [Climate change in Africa § Agriculture](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change_in_Africa#Agriculture "Climate change in Africa").\[ [edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Climate_change_in_Africa&action=edit)\]

Agriculture is a particularly important sector in Africa, contributing towards livelihoods and economies across the continent. Many people rely on the agriculture as a way to build themselves economically and a source of food. Many rely on climate sensitive resources. It is projected that crop activity will decrease across Sub-Saharan Africa.[\[205\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-205) On average, agriculture in Sub-Saharan Africa contributes 15% of the total GDP.[\[206\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-OECD/FAO-206) Africa's geography makes it particularly vulnerable to climate change, and 70% of the population rely on [rain-fed agriculture](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rainfed_agriculture "Rainfed agriculture") for their livelihoods.[\[207\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-207) [Smallholder](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Smallholding "Smallholding") farms account for 80% of cultivated lands in Sub-Saharan Africa.[\[206\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-OECD/FAO-206) The IPCC in 2007 projected that climate variability and change would severely compromise agricultural productivity and access to food.[\[208\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Climate_change_in_Africa_ar4_summary_of_regional_impacts-208): 13  This projection was assigned "high confidence". Cropping systems, livestock and fisheries will be at greater risk of pest and diseases as a result of future climate change.[\[209\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Climate_change_in_Africa_Dhanush-209) Crop pests already account for approximately 1/6th of farm productivity losses.[\[209\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Climate_change_in_Africa_Dhanush-209) Climate change will accelerate the prevalence of pests and diseases and increase the occurrence of highly impactful events.[\[209\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Climate_change_in_Africa_Dhanush-209) The impacts of climate change on agricultural production in Africa will have serious implications for food security and livelihoods. The lack of diverse nutrition caused a failure to ingest certain essential nutrition. The lack of these nutrition can cause deficiency in vitamin A and iron. This was very common to see during the times of droughts and lack of foods because options were few. [\[210\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-210)Between 2014 and 2018, Africa had the highest levels of food insecurity in the world.[\[211\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-211)

In relation to agricultural systems, heavy reliance on rain-fed subsistence farming and low adoption of [climate smart agricultural practices](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_smart_agriculture "Climate smart agriculture") contribute to the sector's high levels of vulnerability. The situation is compounded by poor reliability of, and access to, climate data and information to support adaptation actions.[\[212\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-212) Observed and projected disruptions in precipitation patterns due to climate change are likely to shorten growing seasons and affect crop yield in many parts of Africa. Furthermore, the agriculture sector in Africa is dominated by smallholder farmers with limited access to technology and the resources to adapt.[\[213\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-213)

Climate variability and change have been and continue to be the principal source of fluctuations in global food production across developing countries where production is highly rain-dependent.[\[214\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-214)[\[215\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-215) The agriculture sector is sensitive to climate variability,[\[216\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-216) especially the inter-annual variability of precipitation, temperature patterns, and extreme weather events (droughts and floods). These climatic events are predicted to increase in the future and are expected to have significant consequences to the agriculture sector.[\[217\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Climate_change_in_Africa_Sabiiti_et_al_2018_Adapting_Agriculture_to_Climate_Change-217) This would have a negative influence on food prices, food security, and land-use decisions.[\[218\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-218) Yields from [rainfed agriculture](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rainfed_agriculture "Rainfed agriculture") in some African countries could be reduced by up to 50% by 2020.[\[217\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Climate_change_in_Africa_Sabiiti_et_al_2018_Adapting_Agriculture_to_Climate_Change-217) To prevent the future destructive impact of climate variability on food production, it is crucial to adjust or suggest possible policies to cope with increased climate variability. African countries need to build a national legal framework to manage food resources in accordance with the anticipated climate variability. However, before devising a policy to cope with the impacts of climate variability, especially to the agriculture sector, it is critical to have a clear understanding of how climate variability affects different food crops.[\[219\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-219) This is particularly relevant in 2020 due to the severe invasion of [Locusts](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Locust "Locust") adversely affecting agriculture in eastern Africa.[\[220\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Climate_change_in_Africa_Rosane-2020-220) The invasion was partially attributed to climate change – the warmer temperature and heavier rainfall which caused an abnormal increase in the number of locusts.[\[220\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Climate_change_in_Africa_Rosane-2020-220)

### Asia

\[ [edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture&action=edit&section=39 "Edit section: Asia")\]

For [East](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/East_Asia "East Asia") and [Southeast Asia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Southeast_Asia "Southeast Asia"), an estimate in 2007 stated that [crop yields](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Crop_yield "Crop yield") could increase up to 20% by the mid-21st century.[\[144\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-ar4_summary_of_regional_impacts-144): 13  In [Central](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Central_Asia "Central Asia") and South Asia, projections suggested that yields might decrease by up to 30%, over the same time period. Taken together, the risk of hunger was projected to remain very high in several developing countries.\[ _[needs update](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers#Chronological_items "Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers")_\]

Different Asian Countries have various effects from climate change. [China](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/China "China"), for example, benefits from a 1.5 °C (2.7 °F) temperature increase scenario accompanying with carbon fertilization and leading to a 3% gain of US$18 billion per year; however, [India](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/India "India") will face two thirds of the continent's aggregate losses on agriculture because its high corp net revenue suffers from the high spring temperature.[\[221\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-221) In the [Indo-Gangetic plain](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Indo-Gangetic_Plain "Indo-Gangetic Plain") of India, heat stress and water availability are predicted to have significant negative effects on yield of wheat.[\[222\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-222) Direct effects of increased mean and maximum temperatures is predicted to reduce wheat yields by up to 10%. The effect of reduced availability of water for irrigation is more significant, running at yield losses up to 35%.

Due to climate change, [livestock](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Livestock "Livestock") production will be decreased in [Bangladesh](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bangladesh "Bangladesh") by diseases, scarcity of forage, heat stress and breeding strategies.[\[223\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-223)

### Australia and New Zealand

\[ [edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture&action=edit&section=40 "Edit section: Australia and New Zealand")\]

Without further adaptation to climate change, projected effects would likely be substantial. By 2030, production from agriculture and [forestry](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Forestry "Forestry") was projected to decline over much of southern and eastern Australia, and over parts of eastern New Zealand.[\[224\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-hennessy_australia_and_new_zealand-224) In New Zealand, initial benefits were projected close to major rivers and in western and southern areas.[\[224\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-hennessy_australia_and_new_zealand-224)

### Europe

\[ [edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture&action=edit&section=41 "Edit section: Europe")\]

For [Southern Europe](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Southern_Europe "Southern Europe"), it was predicted in 2007 that climate change would reduce crop productivity.[\[144\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-ar4_summary_of_regional_impacts-144): 14  In [Central](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Central_Europe "Central Europe") and [Eastern Europe](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Eastern_Europe "Eastern Europe"), forest productivity was expected to decline. In [Northern Europe](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Northern_Europe "Northern Europe"), the initial effect of climate change was projected to increase crop yields. The 2019 [European Environment Agency](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/European_Environment_Agency "European Environment Agency") report "Climate change adaptation in the agricultural sector in Europe" again confirmed this. According to this 2019 report, projections indicate that yields of non-irrigated crops like wheat, corn and sugar beet would decrease in southern Europe by up to 50% by 2050 (under a high-end emission scenario). This could result in a substantial decrease in farm income by that date. Also farmland values are projected to decrease in parts of southern Europe by more than 80% by 2100, which could result in land abandonment. The trade patterns are also said to be affected, in turn affecting agricultural income. Also, increased food demand worldwide could exert pressure on food prices in the coming decades.[\[225\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-225) In [Ukraine](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ukraine "Ukraine"), where temperatures are increasing throughout the year and precipitation is predicted to increase, [winter wheat](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Winter_wheat "Winter wheat") yields (wheat sown in winter) could increase by 20–40% in the north and northwestern regions by 2050, as compared to 2010.[\[226\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-226)

### Latin America

\[ [edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture&action=edit&section=42 "Edit section: Latin America")\]

See also: [Climate justice § Examples](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_justice#Examples "Climate justice")

The major agricultural products of [Latin America](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Latin_American "Latin American") include [livestock](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Livestock "Livestock") and grains; such as maize, wheat, soybeans, and rice.[\[227\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-ref_1-227)[\[228\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-ref_2-228) Increased temperatures and altered hydrological cycles are predicted to translate to shorter growing seasons, overall reduced biomass production, and lower grain yields.[\[228\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-ref_2-228)[\[229\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-ref_3-229) [Brazil](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Brazil "Brazil"), [Mexico](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mexico "Mexico") and [Argentina](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Argentina "Argentina") alone contribute 70-90% of the total agricultural production in Latin America.[\[228\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-ref_2-228) In these and other dry regions, maize production is expected to decrease.[\[227\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-ref_1-227)[\[228\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-ref_2-228) A study summarising a number of impact studies of climate change on agriculture in Latin America indicated that wheat is expected to decrease in Brazil, Argentina and [Uruguay](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Uruguay "Uruguay").[\[228\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-ref_2-228) Livestock, which is the main agricultural product for parts of Argentina, Uruguay, southern Brazil, [Venezuela](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Venezuela "Venezuela"), and [Colombia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Colombia "Colombia") is likely to be reduced.[\[227\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-ref_1-227)[\[228\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-ref_2-228) Variability in the degree of production decrease among different regions of Latin America is likely.[\[227\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-ref_1-227) For example, one 2003 study that estimated future maize production in Latin America predicted that by 2055 maize in eastern Brazil will have moderate changes while Venezuela is expected to have drastic decreases.[\[227\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-ref_1-227)

Increased rainfall variability has been one of the most devastating consequences of climate change in Central America and Mexico. From 2009 to 2019, the region saw years of heavy rainfall in between years of below average rainfall.[\[230\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-:8-230) The spring rains of May and June have been particularly erratic, posing issues for farmers plant their maize crops at the onset of the spring rains. Most subsistence farmers in the region have no irrigation and thus depend on the rains for their crops to grow. In Mexico, only 21% of farms are irrigated, leaving the remaining 79% dependent on rainfall.[\[231\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-:9-231)

Suggested potential adaptation strategies to mitigate the effects of global warming on agriculture in Latin America include using plant breeding technologies and installing irrigation infrastructure.[\[228\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-ref_2-228)

### North America

\[ [edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture&action=edit&section=43 "Edit section: North America")\]

See also: [Climate change and agriculture in the United States](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change_and_agriculture_in_the_United_States "Climate change and agriculture in the United States")

[![Four political leaders sit on a stage facing an audience. They are answering questions at a food security and climate change session.](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/U.S.-Africa_Leaders_Summit_Food_Security_%26_Climate_Change_Session.jpg/250px-U.S.-Africa_Leaders_Summit_Food_Security_%26_Climate_Change_Session.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:U.S.-Africa_Leaders_Summit_Food_Security_%26_Climate_Change_Session.jpg) U.S. and African leaders meet at a Leaders Summit for Food Security and Climate change at the National Academy of Sciences in Washington, D.C. in 2014.

Droughts are becoming more frequent and intense in arid and [semiarid](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Semiarid "Semiarid") western North America as temperatures have been rising, advancing the timing and magnitude of spring snow melt floods and reducing river flow volume in summer.[\[232\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-232) Direct effects of climate change include increased heat and water stress, altered crop [phenology](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Phenology "Phenology"), and disrupted symbiotic interactions. These effects may be exacerbated by climate changes in river flow, and the combined effects are likely to reduce the abundance of native trees in favour of non-native [herbaceous](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Herbaceous "Herbaceous") and drought-tolerant competitors, reduce the habitat quality for many native animals, and slow litter decomposition and [nutrient](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nutrient "Nutrient") cycling. Climate change effects on human water demand and irrigation may intensify these effects.[\[233\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-233)

In [Canada](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Canada "Canada"), notable increases are predicted for spring-sown wheat.[\[234\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-234)

## Adaptation

\[ [edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture&action=edit&section=44 "Edit section: Adaptation")\]

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Climate_smart_agriculture_in_Machakos_county_%2815453997230%29.jpg/250px-Climate_smart_agriculture_in_Machakos_county_%2815453997230%29.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Climate_smart_agriculture_in_Machakos_county_(15453997230).jpg) [Climate smart agriculture](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_smart_agriculture "Climate smart agriculture") in [Machakos County](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Machakos_County "Machakos County"), Kenya

Climate change adaptation measures may reduce the risk of negative effects on agriculture from climate change. Adaptation can occur through changes in management practices, agricultural innovation, institutional changes, and [climate-smart agriculture](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate-smart_agriculture "Climate-smart agriculture").[\[235\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-235) To create a [sustainable food system](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sustainable_food_system "Sustainable food system"), these measures are considered as essential as changes needed to reduce global warming in general.[\[236\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-236)[\[237\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-237)

Agricultural innovation is essential to addressing the potential issues of climate change. This includes better management of soil, water-saving technology, matching crops to environments, introducing different crop varieties, crop rotations, appropriate [fertilization](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fertilization "Fertilization") use, and supporting community-based adaptation strategies.[\[202\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Beddington-202)[\[238\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-IFPRI-238) On a government and global level, research and investments into [agricultural productivity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Agricultural_productivity "Agricultural productivity") and [infrastructure](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Infrastructure "Infrastructure") must be done to get a better picture of the issues involved and the best methods to address them. [Government policies](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Government_policies "Government policies") and programs must provide environmentally sensitive government [subsidies](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Subsidies "Subsidies"), educational campaigns, and economic incentives as well as funds, [insurance](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Insurance "Insurance"), and safety nets for vulnerable populations.[\[239\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Hertel-239)[\[202\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Beddington-202)[\[128\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Chakra-128) In addition, providing [early warning systems](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Early_warning_systems "Early warning systems"), and accurate [weather forecasts](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Weather_forecasts "Weather forecasts") to poor or remote areas will allow for better preparation.[\[202\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Beddington-202)

This section is an excerpt from [Climate change adaptation § Changed rainfall patterns in agriculture](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change_adaptation#Changed_rainfall_patterns_in_agriculture "Climate change adaptation").\[ [edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Climate_change_adaptation&action=edit)\]

Climate change is altering global rainfall patterns. This affects agriculture.[\[240\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Climate_change_adaptation_cep-jennings-240) [Rainfed agriculture](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rainfed_agriculture "Rainfed agriculture") accounts for 80% of global agriculture.[\[241\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-241) Many of the 852 million poor people in the world live in parts of Asia and Africa that depend on rainfall to cultivate food crops. Climate change will modify [rainfall](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rainfall "Rainfall"), [evaporation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evaporation "Evaporation"), [runoff](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Surface_runoff "Surface runoff"), and [soil moisture](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soil_moisture "Soil moisture") storage. Extended drought can cause the failure of small and marginal farms. This results in increased economic, political and social disruption.

Water availability strongly influences all kinds of agriculture. Changes in total [seasonal precipitation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seasonal_precipitation "Seasonal precipitation") or its pattern of variability are both important. [Moisture stress](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moisture_stress "Moisture stress") during flowering, pollination, and grain-filling harms most crops. It is particularly harmful to corn, soybeans, and wheat. Increased [evaporation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evaporation "Evaporation") from the soil and accelerated [transpiration](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Transpiration "Transpiration") in the plants themselves will cause moisture stress.

There are many adaptation options. One is to develop crop varieties with greater [drought tolerance](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Drought_tolerance "Drought tolerance")[\[242\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-242) and another is to build local rainwater storage. Using small planting basins to harvest water in Zimbabwe has boosted maize yields. This happens whether rainfall is [abundant](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abundant_number "Abundant number") or scarce. And in Niger they have led to three or fourfold increases in millet yields.[\[243\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-243)

Digital technologies allow farmers to adapt to changing rainfall patterns through remote sensing of soil moisture, IoT-based irrigation control, and data analytics for rainfall forecasting.[\[244\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-244)

Climate change can threaten food security and [water security](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Water_security "Water security"). It is possible to adapt food systems to improve food security and prevent negative impacts from climate change in the future.[\[245\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-245)

A 2023 study published in Nature Food highlights that climate change poses severe risks to global food security, and emphasizes that urgent adaptation and mitigation strategies are needed to protect vulnerable populations and food systems worldwide.[\[246\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-246)

## Greenhouse gas emissions from agriculture

\[ [edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture&action=edit&section=45 "Edit section: Greenhouse gas emissions from agriculture")\]

This section is an excerpt from [Greenhouse gas emissions from agriculture](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Greenhouse_gas_emissions_from_agriculture "Greenhouse gas emissions from agriculture").\[ [edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Greenhouse_gas_emissions_from_agriculture&action=edit)\]

[Greenhouse gas emissions from agriculture](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Greenhouse_gas_emissions_from_agriculture "Greenhouse gas emissions from agriculture") are large: the agriculture, forestry and land use sectors contribute between 13% and 21% of global [greenhouse gas emissions](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Greenhouse_gas_emissions "Greenhouse gas emissions").[\[247\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Greenhouse_gas_emissions_from_agriculture_:0-247) _Direct_ greenhouse gas emissions include those from [rice](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rice "Rice") and [livestock](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Livestock "Livestock") farming.[\[248\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-248) _Indirect_ emissions from the conversion of non-agricultural land such as [forests](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Forest "Forest") into agricultural land are also very important.[\[249\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-249)[\[250\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-250) With regard to direct emissions, [nitrous oxide](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nitrous_oxide "Nitrous oxide") and [methane](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Methane "Methane") makeup over half of total greenhouse gas emissions from agriculture.[\[251\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-Greenhouse_gas_emissions_from_agriculture_FAO2020-251) A 2023 review emphasizes that emissions from agricultural soils are shaped by factors such as soil type, climate, and management practices. It also highlights several mitigation strategies, including conservation tillage, precision agriculture, improved water use, and the application of biochar, that can reduce emissions and enhance soil carbon storage.[\[252\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture#cite_note-252)

[Climate Action Tracker](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_Action_Tracker "Climate Action Tracker") predicts 2.5 to 2.9 °C of warming based on current policies and action, with pledges and government agreements taking this to 2.1 °C.

We show a persistent and widespread increase of growing season integrated LAI (greening) over 25% to 50% of the global vegetated area, whereas less than 4% of the globe shows decreasing LAI (browning). Factorial simulations with multiple global ecosystem models suggest that CO2 fertilization effects explain 70% of the observed greening trend

Title: Climate Changes Health: Food and Agriculture
[Skip To Content](https://www.apha.org/topics-and-issues/climate-health-and-equity/agriculture#main)

![](https://www.apha.org/img/decor-dots-xs.png)

# Climate Changes Health: Food and Agriculture

_"We tend to blame people who waste food, farm in unsustainable ways or are obese, but we need to understand what is really going on. We need practical solutions, and we also need a broad social commitment to address root causes." --_ Roni Neff, PhD, MS, The Johns Hopkins Center for a Livable Future

Climate change is expected to threaten food production, food quality, food prices and distribution systems. Many crop yields are predicted to decline because of the combined effects of change in precipitation, more frequent and severe weather events and increasing competition from weeds and pests on crop plants. Livestock and fish production are also projected to decline. Prices are expected to rise in response to declining food production and such trends as costlier pesticides, fertilizers and the petroleum used for agricultural machinery and the distribution of food.

These trends affect health in several ways:

- Americans with particular dietary patterns, such as Native Americans, will confront **shortages of key heritage foods** like corn, wild rice, squash and beans. Today, 60 percent of U.S. counties with a native majority face dangerously high food insecurity rates. With food insecurity and scarcity come higher food prices. Vulnerable populations then cope by turning to nutrient-poor but calorie-rich foods, with consequences ranging from malnutrition to diabetes to obesity.
- The **nutritional value of some foods is declining**. Rising temperatures, variable climates and higher carbon dioxide levels in the air decrease the amount of zinc, iron, protein and other nutrients in the crops much of the world's poor depend upon. Those crops include rice, maize and wheat.
- Higher temperatures will **encourage higher rates of microbial growth, affecting food safety**. Farmers expect to apply more herbicides and pesticides because of an increase in pests, insects, disease and weeds. That will lead to increased exposure to potentially toxic residues among famers, farmworkers and consumers.
- **Water-related aspects of climate change** include scarcity of water for irrigation due to draught and contamination of water due to floods.
- Higher temperatures and more frequent periods of heat may result in **more cases of heat-related illness** (heat stroke and heat exhaustion) and fatigue for farmers, ranchers, migrant workers and other agricultural workers.

Research is shifting from climate change's impacts on food production to ways we can adapt to and ease these impacts. We support systems, policies and communities that meet this challenge, improving food safety and security for all of us.

Follow the conversation using the hashtag [#ClimateChangesHealth](https://twitter.com/search?q=%23ClimateChangesHealth&src=typd)
